Executive Summary 
Historically, the literature on due diligence performed in Mergers & Acquisitions (M&As) has 
covered the legal, commercial, and financial aspects of an acquisition target. However, continuous 
growth in M&A activities and a pivotal shift toward operational value creation as the primary driver 
for M&A success, demands a structured and efficient approach to assess a target company’s 
operating model in the pre-acquisition phase.   
 
Despite extensive research attention to M&A and the associated due diligence process, the operational 
assessment performed before merging or acquiring a company is subject to limited research. 
Historically, scientific work has primarily been carried out for the overall success in M&A. The 
research tends to focus on the commercial and organizational aspects of the post-acquisition phase, 
however M&A practitioners acknowledge the criticality and difficulty of performing an operational 
due diligence in the pre-acquisition phase. While practitioners request a structured process to assess 
a target company’s operating model, no framework or approach exist in extant literature. This master 
thesis is used to address the research gap and strengthen the comprehension of operational due 
diligence in M&A. Lastly, the thesis presents a framework to scope and structure the operational due 
diligence process for practitioners. 
 
Three research methods are used to address the research gap: 1) an extensive literature study of more 
than 130 academic and business publications, 2) 11 explorative and in-depth interviews with both 
M&A investors and advisors, and 3) a survey questionnaire with 28 respondents to obtain a broader 
perspective on the operational due diligence drivers and analyses.   
 
The research resulted in two key findings on how to scope and structure the operational due diligence. 
Firstly, the scope of the operational due diligence is based on the four fundamental supply chain 
elements: Sourcing & Procurement, Production & Manufacturing, Inventory & Warehousing, and 
Distribution & Logistics. This scope ensures a holistic and exhaustive approach and help inclusion 
an end-to-end perspective of the value chain processes. Secondly, the thesis identified five distinct 
operational deal drivers: Operational Maturity, Improvement Potentials, Scalability, Operational 
Risks, and Synergies. Each driver is used to perform a structured assessment of the target company 
and the outcome hereof has a direct influence on the final decision to complete the merger or 
acquisition.  
 
An Operational Due Diligence Framework was developed iteratively throughout the research process 
as new insights emerged from the literature review, interviews, and survey questionnaire. The 
framework incorporates the four supply chain elements and the five operational deal drivers. 
Practically, it is applied in an iterative and hypothesis-driven process consisting of four steps: 1) 
Initialize, 2) Hypothesize, 3) Analyze, and 4) Evaluate. The framework and process encourage a 
structured and progression-focused approach, which strengthens the practitioner’s ability to reach 
accurate and fast conclusions. 
 
The dissertation contributes to the extant literature with five novel operational deal drivers, and 
enlarge the research on the subject in general. In addition, the paper provides practitioners with a 
normative framework to support the execution of an operational due diligence and present three 
findings that leverage the probability for a successful operational due diligence.

Preface 
Our collective experience from multiple operational due diligence projects triggered our deep interest 
in the topic. This interest made operational due diligence an obvious theme for our thesis. Now, after 
having researched for more than a year, it is our sincere belief that this thesis will contribute to the 
topic of operational due diligence both in academia and in practice. Surprisingly, and despite 
extensive research on M&A in general, limited extant literature dealt with operational due diligence 
in M&A. An early identification of the apparent research gap intensified our interest to explore 
operational due diligence as a research topic. 
 
As we have learned throughout the research process, most of the due diligence efforts have 
historically been assigned to the financial, commercial and legal assessment of a target company. 
Consequently, many merging or acquiring companies experience unfortunate issues associated with 
the target company’s operating model, after having closed the deal. While more focus on the 
operational aspects could have identified and mitigated these problems. Still today, many M&A 
practitioners do not perform operational due diligence in the pre-acquisition phase, either because 
they don’t know how to, don’t have the necessary capabilities, or they are unaware of the negative 
consequences of neglecting an operational assessment. The result of either ignoring operational due 
diligence entirely or performing it insufficiently has two critical outcomes: 1) Unidentified issues 
emerge in the post-acquisition phase, resulting in an overvalued target company. 2) Unidentified 
potentials are not included in the valuation of the target company, and the merger or acquisition is 
discarded due to insufficient return on investment. As expressed by an M&A practitioner; “the two 
outcomes are equally bad, as you don’t want any nasty surprises, but you don’t want to walk away 
from a good investment case either.” Accordingly, we do believe that the subject needs more attention 
in research and in practice.  
 
This thesis is structured into two articles with the purpose to publish the material separately in 
different journals. The first article serves to contribute to the academic field, while the second article 
contributes to a business-oriented segment. Specifically, we have selected the Journal of International 
Management and Harvard Business Review. Practically, this explains the two articles different 
format, style of writing, and focus areas.  
 
Throughout the research process, our findings have been presented at the European Operations 
Management Conference in Edinburgh 2017, and at the 22nd International Symposium on Logistics 
in Slovenia 2017. On both occasions, the presentations received significant attention and resulted in 
constructive feedback which subsequently have been incorporated in the thesis. 
 
 
 
 
 
Chris Berg Porsgaard 
 
Mathias Haubjerg 
 
 
 
 
Kongens Lyngby, January 2018

1. Introduction 
Ever since humanity began doing business, Mergers & Acquisitions (M&As) have existed in its 
simplest form – where one farmer acquired another farmer’s “business” or agriculture. However, 
M&A as we know it today, emerged in the 19th century in the US as smaller businesses consolidated 
to form larger and more powerful entities with the primary purpose of dominating their markets. 
During this time, companies such as Dupont, US Steel, and General Electric secured their leading 
market positions through M&A activities – which still is a core part of their business strategy 
(Lamoreaux, 1985). Today, M&A is a trillion-dollar industry and more than 50,000 mergers and 
acquisitions were completed in 2017. Business media is daily reporting M&A speculations and 
transactions such as Walt Disney’s 50-billion-dollar acquisition of the mass media corporation 21st 
Century Fox (Gartenberg, 2017).  
 
There are multiple reasons why a company should either merge or acquire another business. 
Achieving economies of scale, growing in size, eliminating a competitor, attaining tax benefits, 
entering a new market, or getting access to new technology are some examples of the many reasons. 
However, the objective to increase shareholder value through inorganic growth is equivalent in all 
these reasons. Despite the many reasons and ways to improve shareholder value through M&A 
activities, up to 70% fail to realize the desired goals (Wik, 2017). Roberts et al. (2017) point to 
operational due diligence as the primary lever for better alignment of operational potentials and 
objectives. Furthermore, there is a high correlation between successful M&As and the ability to 
accurately perform operational due diligence of a target company prior the acquisition. Performing 
the operational due diligence allows the acquirer to identify and evaluate operational improvements 
and risks avoiding an undesirable situation. Hence, clarifying the deal makers and deal breakers. 
Furthermore, nine out of ten M&A professionals attribute operational improvements and cost 
reductions as the number one strategic lever for value creation (MacArthur et al., 2017). 
Consequently, this thesis is dedicated to increase the understanding of the operational deal drivers 
and to develop a normative framework to assist M&A practitioners in conducting operational due 
diligence. 
 
2. Scope 
This section clarifies the purpose of the thesis and helps the reader understand central terms and 
concepts used throughout the articles.  
Purpose of the Thesis 
The first object of the thesis is to understand the drivers for operational due diligence in M&A. 
Secondly, the thesis seeks to identify a structured approach for how to perform operational due 
diligence with greater success. Both purposes are addressed through a combination of literature 
research and empirical studies with the intent to clarify both academic and practical aspects of the 
subject. Due to limited extant literature and studies on the subject, explorative research has been 
decisive throughout the process and in the outcome. Accordingly, empirical research was central to 
obtain knowledge about best practice from M&A practitioners. The empirical studies were restricted 
to focus on operational due diligence cases for privately-held companies with a production of physical 
goods, and a global footprint. Conclusively, the objective is to enlarge the research on the operational 
due diligence and to obtain practical knowledge on the subject.  
==PAGE:12==
Master Thesis: Operational Due Diligence Framework 
 
12 
 
Research Questions 
This thesis attempt to answer two primary research questions in an academic context and a practical 
business context. The two research questions are:  
  
1. “What are the operational drivers influencing the acquisition decision for acquiring firms in the 
due diligence phase?” 
 
2. “Can theoretical and practical knowledge on operational due diligence be synthesized into a 
normative framework supporting the process of assessing a target company?” 
 
Clarification of Central Terms 
This section presents two overall terms, to establish an unambiguous understanding of the 
fundamental terms used in this paper. Terms such as M&A and Operational Due Diligence are 
referred to in many ways. Hence the clarification and a precise definition is necessary. 
Mergers & Acquisitions  
According to Straub (2007), the term mergers and acquisitions refers to the nature of corporate 
strategy, corporate finance, and management’s activity of buying, selling, and incorporating another 
company with the potential of creating rapid growth for the acquiring company in a given industry. 
As the name implies, mergers and acquisitions essentially describe two different practices.  
 
The first, a merger, is the consolidation of two or more legal entities’ assets and liabilities combined 
into one legal entity (Straub, 2007). During the merger, process negation between the influence 
companies usually occurs, which normally not emerge in an acquisition. Another key differentiator 
is the synergies that two companies can achieve by merging (Roberts et al., 2012). A third key element 
in mergers concerns the cultural fit between two merging organizations. This element is considered 
a high priority element of having a successful merger. 
 
An acquisition is the purchase of shares or assets of another company, with the purpose of achieving 
managerial and strategic influence (Bragg, 2007, Grinblatt et al., 2006 & Straub, 2007). According 
to Bragg (2007), the objective of an acquisition is to keep the two involved companies as separate 
legal entities after the transaction is realized. Hence the firms operate as standalone companies. 
However, Grinblatt et al. (2011) and Straub (2007) state that the two involved entities do not 
necessarily have to be set up as two separate companies after the transaction. In contrary, the two 
entities can amalgamate into one corporation. This contradicts Bragg’s (2007) definition of an 
acquisition. For the purpose of this paper, it is chosen to use Bragg’s (2007) definition of an 
acquisition, as this is considered the most common understanding. 
 
According to Robert, et al. (2012), an acquisition intends to make a profitable sale of the target 
company. Malik and Anuar (2014) propose that the primary objective of entering a merger and 
acquisition deal is to become more beneficial to a greater composition, two or more companies 
working together. Furthermore, the bigger perspective of an organization is to increase the 
shareholders’ dividends, where M&A is a supporting instrument to enter new potential markets or 
new business areas or a stronger position in existing markets. 
==PAGE:13==
Master Thesis: Operational Due Diligence Framework 
13 
Many different motivation factors are mentioned in the literature. Kishore (2009), listed top motives 
to promote M&A. The five most important are listed in order of their priority, beginning with the 
most important: 
  
• 
“Take advantage of awareness that a company is undervalued.”  
• 
“Achieve growth more rapidly by the internal effort.” 
• 
“Satisfy market demand for additional products/service.” 
• 
“Avoid risks of internal start-ups of expansion.” 
• 
“Increasing earnings per share.” 
M&A is used as generic term for mergers and acquisitions throughout this research paper. 
Furthermore, the M&A term will be used to describe both merger and acquisition activity, even 
though most M&A activity constitutes of acquisitions (Hubbard et al., 2008). As operational due 
diligence is incorporated as an element in the M&A process, it is essential to understand the affiliation 
and the interaction between due diligence and M&A. The M&A process is in the literature described 
in various ways. An early study by Boland (1970) introduced a two-step M&A process composing of 
a pre-merger and a post-merger phase. Salus (1989) proposed the third step, and the M&A process 
changed to pre-merger, merger, and post-merger. A new procedure for the M&A process was 
introduced by Carpenter and Sanders (2007). This process is divided into four phases: Idea, 
Justification (including due diligence and deal negotiation), Acquisition Integration, and Results 
Analysis. The more popular model was the Watson Wyatt Deal Flow Model presented by Galpin and 
Herndon (2000). The model divides the process into five steps: Formulate, Locate, Investigate, 
Negotiate, and Integrate. This paper refers to Koerner’s (2013) model, which consist of five stages 
with similar phases as Galpin and Herndon (2000), but with slightly different terminology, namely 
Strategy, Screening, Due Diligence, Negotiation, and Integration and Execution. 
Due Diligence & Operational Due Diligence  
The due diligence process is an underlying part of the M&A process. However, since many different 
definitions exist on operational due diligence, it is important to clarify the extent and purpose. Due 
diligence in the M&A process refers to the process where the acquirer and its advisors, review and 
analyze an acquisition candidate. This will secure that the subsequent purchase of the company 
satisfies the expectations and that risk is exposed in the early stages (Lajoux, 2010). According to 
Howson (2003), the due diligence helps the acquirer to identify issues which feed into price 
negotiations. Furthermore, it helps reduce the risk of paying too much for the target company and de-
risk the deal by identifying points, such as legal protection that should be sought. Howson (2003) 
outline five steps of due diligence: Verification of Assets and Liabilities, Identification and 
Quantification of Risks, Protection against Risks, Identification of Synergy Benefits, and Post-
Acquisition Planning. Xiao (2013) present another approach, by divide the due diligence process into 
six steps as illustrated in Table 1.

For the purpose of this paper, it is not relevant to elaborate on the first four and the last step of Xiao 
model (2013) but focus on the fifth. In the fifth step (Full-Scale Due Diligence), commercial, 
financial, legal, and operational aspects of the target company are reviewed and assessed based on 
available data and information. Furthermore, the advisory team including auditors, legal counsels, 
and consultants (e.g., operational experts) get access to site visits and interviews, which is particularly 
important for the operational aspect of the due diligence (Herd, 2004).  
Many different types of due diligence exist, and especially three types are widespread in the literature; 
Financial Due Diligence, Legal Due Diligence, and Commercial Due Diligence (Howson, 2003). 
However, there are several other due diligence; Human resources and Culture, Management, Tax, 
Environmental, IT, Technical, Operational, Intellectual Property Rights, Antitrust and 
Insurance/Risk. According to Grebey (2012), the three most important due diligence is Financial, 
Legal and Operational. Also, Rothenbücher (2008) argue that operational and business knowledge 
has become the root to successful M&A’s. The operational due diligence provides this in-deep 
understanding of the operating model and insights into the target company’s operational value. Figure 
1 illustrates a high-level breakdown of the M&A and Due Diligence process.

No well-established method is defined in the extant literature to support the operational due diligence, 
while there exist proper assessments for commercial, financial and legal principles (Grebey, 2012). 
Hence this work will focus on operational due diligence to enlarge academic and practical knowledge 
on the subject.

3. Article 1: The Academic Article 
The first article takes an academic perspective and investigates the extant literature on operational 
due diligence. To comprehend the inadequate literature, additional interviews and a survey support 
the study. In Appendix C interview guidelines, e-mails, interview results, etc. are enclosed, while 
Appendix E contains the transcribed and coded interview documentation.

The key to success in operational due diligence: 
A framework for practitioners 
Chris Berg Porsgaard*,a,1, Mathias Haubjerg*,b,2,  
Zaza Nadja Lee Herbert-Hansenc,3 
a,b,cDepartment of Management Engineering, Technical University of Denmark,  
Produktionstorvet Bygning 426, Kgs. Lyngby 2800, Denmark 
 
Abstract 
Operational due diligence is an essential step in the acquisition decision process when private equity firm assess whether 
a target company can realize future performance expectations. The scope of this paper is to identify operational due 
diligence determinants and develop a normative framework to support acquisition practitioners in the due diligence 
process. A structured and comprehensive literature review within the field of operational due diligence was carried out 
with emphasis on identification of operational determinants within academic research. In addition, 15 interviews and an 
explorative survey questionnaire helped establish an empirical foundation for the research leading to novel findings. This 
paper presents five operational drivers all considered as having a direct influence on the operational part of an acquisition 
decision, which is identified across extant literature and empirical data. The operational drivers are structured in a supply 
chain based framework to simplify the practical application. Furthermore, the five drivers collectively comprise 20 sets 
of analyses and determinants specifically for addressing each operational driver hypothesis. Through the literature review, 
a significant research gap is identified for both the operational due diligence framework and the underlying components 
influencing the acquisition decision. However, empirical data in terms of interviews and a survey questionnaire 
supplemented the literature and helped establish the operational due diligence framework and associated application 
procedure. Most research within mergers and acquisitions, and due diligence focus on either the legal, financial, or 
strategic aspects of the acquisition decision made when an acquirer purchases a target company. Hence, this paper 
suggests a novel framework for assessing the operational drivers, limitations and improvement potentials, about 
performing a successful operational due diligence. 
 
 
 
Keywords: Operational due diligence; Due diligence framework; Pre-Acquisition assessment 
1. Introduction 
The number of mergers and acquisitions (M&A) has over the last five years increased significantly (Institute 
of Mergers, Acquisitions, and Alliances, 2016). While the overall success rate of M&A activity has remained 
low (Sher, 2012), between 70% and 90% are estimated to fail according to studies from professor Roger L. 
Martin (Martin, 2016). The firm instigating the acquisition often fail to identify the acquisition target’s ability 
to improve their current operational setup and ability to support the growth projections early in the due 
diligence process. This problem is caused by a lack of sufficient understanding of the target company’s 
operating model and cost structure (Christensen, 2011, Christofferson et al. 2004). 
Traditionally, the financial and legal aspects of an M&A due diligence have been in focus. This focus means 
that the financial statements, assets, cash flows and contractual obligations are thoroughly examined, leaving 
                                               
* Joint first author: 1 Tel.: + 45-51593925, Email: s104231@student.dtu.dk 2 Tel.: + 45-51803550, Email: s120071@student.dtu.dk 3 Tel.: + 45-

==PAGE:18==
 
C. Porsgaard et al. / Journal of International Management (2018) 
 
Master Thesis: Operational Due Diligence Framework 
18 
 
18 
the operational aspect under prioritized (Shaughnessy, 2009). Consequently, the neglection of operational 
insights signifies that crucial knowledge about the operating model, strengths and weaknesses, and the cost 
structure are omitted as a knowledge determinant in the final acquisition decision. Such insights do not only 
contribute to the actual acquisition decision, but functions as input to the company valuation, a lever for price 
negotiations, and provide knowledge for the following integration and realization of improvement potentials 
(Herd, 2004). It is therefore suggested to put an equal emphasis on both the operational aspect and on the legal 
- and financial part of due diligence (Morrison, 2008). 
As M&A deals become more comprehensive and wide-ranging (Picker, 2016), so does the importance of 
understanding the fundamental drivers of the target company (Herd, 2004). Besides, uncertainty and 
complexity in supply chains, and hereby operations, are simultaneously increasing (Malik, 2011). This 
increase, seen in the intricate distribution network, supplier relations, IT systems, etc. which contributes further 
to the importance of understanding the operational drivers, specifically within manufacturing firms where the 
flow of physical goods and increasing supplier dependencies create complexity. At the same time, M&A 
activity is the highest within the industrial sector such as manufacturing firms, compared to all other sectors 
(Roberts et al., 2016). When considering these two elements, it becomes evident that the aspect of planning 
and effectively performing operational due diligence presents itself as a challenge even for the most 
experienced firms (Recardo, 2014). Therefore, the operational due diligence phase should be considered as 
crucial to validate the target’s performance, potentials, and risks.  Through data collection and analysis leading 
to a comprehensive understanding of deal drivers and improvement opportunity, the phase should be performed 
(Alvarez, 2007). 
 
The general nature of assessing and evaluating an acquisition target is a complicated affair, and unlike 
financial and legal due diligence, there are no principles such as accounting or law to guide operational due 
diligence (Grebey, 2012). Furthermore, it is suggested that a standardized, structured and tested approach 
increases the odds of successful due diligence (Morrison, 2008). However, the research on operational due 
diligence in M&As indicate a substantial research gap. This gap is reflected in the limited amount of qualitative 
and quantitative academic and non-academic publications on the subject.  
To accommodate, the increasing complexity and high industrial M&A activity, the purpose of this paper is 
to develop an operational due diligence framework which can be used to support acquisition decisions for 
acquirers and M&A advisors. This is done by identifying and structuring operational determinants, defined in 
this paper as operational determinants that influence the acquirer’s decision to purchase a specific target 
company. Conclusively, this article seeks to answer the following two research questions: 
 
RQ1: “What are the operational determinants influencing the acquisition decision for acquiring firms in the 
due diligence phase?” 
 
RQ2: ““Can theoretical and practical knowledge on operational due diligence be synthesized into a 
normative framework supporting the process of assessing a target company?” 
 
This paper proposes a guiding framework to support the conduction of operational due diligence. With 
operational determinants identified through a structured literature review and empirical data from practitioner 
interviews the framework present what to investigate in the operational due diligence, while data from both 
practitioner interviews and survey questionnaire provide instruments how to analyze the operational due 
diligence case. 
In this paper, Procurement & Sourcing, Production, Warehousing & Inventory and Transport & Logistics 
constitute the organizational scope of operations due diligence. Although Grebey (2012) consider the 
organizational scope to include Sales, Marketing, and R&D, they are excluded in this paper to build on the 
accustomed operations management elements as defined by (Russell & Taylor, 2016). Furthermore, as 
financial, legal, and commercial aspects of due diligence is out of scope for operational due diligence, these 
aspects are also excluded.  
This paper focuses on firms that produce physical goods, have a global or European footprint, and are 
privately held. Governmental and nonprofit organizations and firms operating in the service sector are therefore 
out of scope. 
This paper is structured into nine main sections. Following the introduction, the second section clarifies two 
terms which are important to the understanding of the paper, namely mergers and acquisitions, and due 
==PAGE:19==

diligence. The third section outlines the applied methodology. The fourth and fifth section constitute the 
literature review and the empirical interview findings. These sections are addressing research question 1. 
Section six presents the survey findings related to research question 2 and hereby testing the proposed ODD 
framework setup. The seventh section synthesizes the literature and empirical findings into a framework. The 
research and findings are subsequently discussed in section eight. Section nine concludes with the papers 
findings, key challenges, and suggestions for further research.  
2. Methodology 
As research on operational due diligence has a complex nature, a qualitative approach is recommended to 
obtain comprehension and in-depth data. The explorative research approach enables a comprehensive 
understanding of the research area. These elements characterize the case-study approach as the most 
appropriate research methodology (Yin, 2009). 
The research is divided into three phases. The first phase explores the operational determinants from both 
academic and empirical perspectives. To study the coherence and distinctions between literature and empirical 
knowledge, a structured comparison hereof was performed. The findings from the extant literature and 
empirical data resulted in an introduction of operational drivers. An explorative survey questionnaire in phase 
two explores which analysis that is typically conducted during the ODD process. Phase one and two enabled 
us to develop a more comprehensive framework in the third phase. Both phase I and II allowed triangularity 
between the extant literature, and the empirical data gathered from the interviews and survey, which enhanced 
the validity of the findings. In Table 1 a structured overview of the three phases is presented.

2.1. Literature Review 
The purpose of conducting the literature review is to identify operational determinants and their influence 
on the operational due diligence. Due to the limited extant literature, this paper seeks to explore both pre-
acquisition and post-acquisition determinants for operational success. Post-acquisition is used to understand 
what influences success after the acquisition and consequently what to investigate and assess in the pre-
acquisition assessment. Hence building on an explorative approach to generate comprehensive understanding.  
As this literature review is the entry study to subsequent empirical research studies of operational due 
diligence, the deductive method is applied to obtain knowledge and insights into the operational due diligence 
process. This approach is chosen as it draws logical findings from the literature to answer the presented 
research question (Saunders et al., 2009). The deductive method can be divided into three phase, (i) theorize 
hypothesis, (ii) data analysis and (iii) hypothesis testing. This literature review is carried out in the first phase, 
where existent literature within the area of operational due diligence is examined. This creates a profound 
knowledge foundation on how to conduct operational due diligence in M&A. 
==PAGE:20==
 
C. Porsgaard et al. / Journal of International Management (2018) 
 
Master Thesis: Operational Due Diligence Framework 
20 
 
20 
The methodology in this paper is primarily based on a structured literature review (Tranfield et al., 2003), 
while the literature review is conducted in accordance with the guidelines that Rowley & Slack (2004) presents. 
Initially, a search was conducted for all relevant terms related to pre-acquisition assessment, post-acquisition 
success and more specifically related to operational due diligence. Two information sources were utilized, 
namely the library database of the Technical University of Denmark, also known as DTU Findit, and 
furthermore Google Scholar, which allows to search across a wide range of academic literature. Based on this 
search approach, 131 numbers of preliminary articles were identified. To discard irrelevant articles, a screening 
process was conducted, with the exclusion criteria outlined in Table 2. The number of articles decreased to 42 
focused articles, being selected from the preliminary ones. Finally, a detailed examination of the remaining 
articles led to a final list of 22 articles, selected for the literature review, which fulfills the criteria for the in-
depth analysis as mentioned in Table 3. In addition, citation pearl growing was used to identify the primary 
authorities within the literature, by investigating relevant articles reference lists.

2.2. Practitioner Interviews 
The purpose of the practitioner interview is twofold. Firstly, the results of the literature review concluded a 
significant research gap, which required additional exploration of the operational due diligence determinants. 
Secondly, the authors emphasize industrial knowledge and insights as a necessity to ensure practical 
application and usability of the concluding result.  
As in the literature research, the interviews are focused on the exploration of operational pre- and post-
acquisition aspects as they support the decisive understanding of operational due diligence determinants. The 
empirical data from the acquires and advisors is based on their prior experience with operational due diligence 
cases. During the research period, the research team worked in close collaboration with the executing part of 
the operational due diligence, namely the advisors. The research was completed in the second quarter of 2017.   
The interviews were designed of two formats, open-ended and semi-structured. The notes were taken to the 
open-ended interviews, while the semi-structured interviews were recorded, and transcribed. The transcription 
of the interviews allowed extraction of relevant information. Table 3 presents a detailed list of the conducted 
interviews.

The empirical data was obtained through semi-structured interviews with acquirers and advisors, and the 
interviewees were selected if they fulfilled multiple criteria. The interviews required no preparation from the 
interviewees, and they were approximately one hour in duration.  
==PAGE:22==
 
C. Porsgaard et al. / Journal of International Management (2018) 
 
Master Thesis: Operational Due Diligence Framework 
22 
 
22 
The criteria refinement helps ensure a high level of relevance and expertise from the interviewees. The 
acquirers were selected based on three key criteria consisting of (i) having performed more than three 
operational due diligence cases, (ii) having performed minimum one operational due diligence in an 
international M&A, (iii) having experience with the acquisition of companies with a production of physical 
goods and a supply chain. Furthermore, to obtain a wide range of perspectives from the acquirers, they were 
chosen based on their acquisition activity level (number of acquisitions per year) and deal volume (average 
cost of acquisition target). The advisors were equally selected based on five criteria were the three first are 
equivalent to three for the acquirers and two additional criteria (iv) having the fundamental understanding of 
financial deal drivers, (v) having deep operational expertise within production and supply chain. In total, 11 
interviews were performed with five acquirers and six operational M&A advisors. Collectively, the 
interviewees have conducted more than 120 operational due diligence cases.  
To better comprehend the outcome of the practitioner interviews, both a coding - and a frequency analysis 
was conducted. The coding analysis placed great emphasis on identifying relevant elements of qualitative data, 
which supports its usage in this papers research (Goodrick, 2014). It was deliberately chosen to utilize a 
formalized coding strategy based on the learnings from the extant literature. This means that the search and 
labeling process of operational determinants took the point of origin in the literary findings. However, not-
previously mentioned terms were included to retain an explorative approach. Lastly, the process required 
critical thinking and reasoning the comprehend interlinkage and coherence in the emerging operational 
determinants. The coding and preliminary frequency analysis resulted in the identification of multiple 
operational determinants across all the qualitative interview data. These are subsequently narrowed down by 
the process of duplicate removal and consolidation of operational determinants. 
2.3. Survey Questionnaire  
The explorative survey aims to identify the most common applied analyses, required to support the 
acquisition decision. In addition, the survey investigates the validity of findings related to the operational due 
diligence framework, hence answering research question 2. The survey questions are developed based on the 
operational determinants and associated analyses identified throughout the literature review and the 
empirical interviews.  
The study was developed and structured into sections based on the approach suggested by Bradbury 
(2004) to ensure a satisfactory outcome. (Bradburn et al., 2004) The sections adapted to the value chain 
perspective identified in the literature review, resulting in the following six sections: 1) Introduction and 
Preliminary 2) Questions, Sourcing & Procurement, 3) Production, 4) Inventory &Warehousing, 5) 
Transport & Logistics, and 6) Closing. The total number of questions was initially 73. These questions 
originated either directly from the literature or the empirical interviews, or were hypothesized from the same 
two sources. A restrictive and iterative process was subsequently performed to identify the most valuable 
survey questions. This process eliminated 24 questions due to overlap, similarity, and insignificance in the 
questions contribution toward identification of analyses. Table 4 provides an overview of the question and 
their relation to the questionnaire sections. 
Table 4. Distribution of survey questions  
Part of survey  
No. of questions 
Introduction 
3 
Sourcing & Procurement 
11 
Production 
11 
Inventory & Warehousing 
11 
Transport & Logistics 
11 
Closing 
2 
Total 
49 
 
The configuration of each question was deliberately chosen to either directly test the relevance of the 
previously identified analyses or to provide additional analyses and knowledge thereof. The initially asked 
==PAGE:23==
 
C. Porsgaard et al. / Journal of International Management (2018) 
 
 
Master Thesis: Operational Due Diligence Framework 
23 
questions were close-ended selection questions, which was supported by open-ended follow-up questions and 
required more detailed answers from the surveys. Examples of questions that are both a selection question and 
an open-ended follow-up question in the survey are: “What do you include when assessing the target 
company's scalability?; Availability of Workforce; Capacity Levels; IT Systems & Documentation; etc.” and 
“If any other elements are included, please elaborate:” Collectively, the explorative survey approach 
contributes to the overall understanding of which analyses should be performed when conducting operational 
due diligence. 
 
To ensure an information-rich and exhaustive questionnaire, the value chain perspective is adapted to the 
survey structure. This aspect means that each section, besides the introduction and closing, structured by 1) 
Sourcing & Procurement, 2) Production, 3) Inventory & Warehousing, and 4) Transport & Logistics. 
Furthermore, the survey is structured in accordance to the operational deal drivers identified in research Phase 
I.  
 
The surveyed population has a global reach. In total 28 respondents completed the survey, of whom 16 % 
are acquirers, and 84 % are advisors with experience in operational due diligence. According to Isaac & 
Michael (1995), a small sample is justified in the context of exploratory research, which fits the chosen 
research methodology in this paper. Furthermore, the results should not be considered as statistically 
significant and should be treated accordingly. However, the results are indicative and contribute as a basis for 
further investigation on the subject of operational due diligence.   
3. Literature Review 
The literature review is divided into three parts. The first part address the motivation for performing 
operational due diligence. The second part presents the literary perspective on the organizational scope of 
operational due diligence. Lastly, the third part examines the operational determinants identified in the extant 
literature. 
3.1. Motivation for Performing Operational Due Diligence 
In the literature, the motivation for performing operational due diligence is considered highly indistinct. 
This motive expresses a fragmented view of the specific motivation, which simultaneously could point to the 
limited historical focus on operational due diligence (Morrison, 2008).  
Roberts et al. (2016) presents that 9 out of 10 M&A practitioners consider operational improvement as the 
most important lever for value creation. In continuation, Recardo (2013) points to profound operational due 
diligence as the key to successful operational value creation. Furthermore, MacArthur et al. (2017) highlights 
that investors often can identify untapped opportunities for operational improvements during the operational 
due diligence. However, this is only feasible if significant time and effort are assigned to obtain a broad 
operational understanding of the target company. The downside of neglecting operational due diligence is 
evident through two acquisition scenarios (MacArthur et al., 2017). The first scenario is the case where the 
acquirer underestimates the operational improvements and overestimates the resource requirement to, e.g., 
risks mitigation, which results in an underbid of the deal, meaning that a good deal may have been lost. The 
second scenario has reversed the situation, where the operational improvements have been overestimated, and 
resource requirements have been underestimated. This will with high likelihood result in a winning bid, but 
the acquirer will struggle with the realization of the operational improvements. Conclusively, the specifics of 
the motivation behind the process of performing an operation due diligence is unexplored in the academic 
literature.  
3.2. Value Chain Perspective 
As suggested by Recardo (2014), one approach to comprehending the acquisition target is from a value 
chain perspective. This method provides an opportunity to assess the individual value chain components from 
suppliers and inbound logistics to marketing, sales, and service. However, for the purpose of this paper, it is 
deliberately chosen to adopt this approach. However, it is also decided to adjust the scope of the operations 
==PAGE:24==
 
C. Porsgaard et al. / Journal of International Management (2018) 
 
Master Thesis: Operational Due Diligence Framework 
24 
 
24 
due diligence to exclude sales, marketing and R&D (Grebey, 2012). Consequently, the resulting organizational 
scope is based on the four following areas: 
 
• 
Sourcing & Procurement: The process of identifying, selecting and purchasing goods and/or services 
from suppliers. 
• 
Production: The process of transforming a tangible input and intangible input into a good or service.   
• 
Inventory & Warehousing: The process of receiving, storing, and packing materials or goods.  
• 
Transport & Logistics: The process of moving goods from A to B, internally in the organization and 
between the organization and the customer.  
 
 
Recardo (2014) further highlights this perspective as a method to mitigate the risk of neglecting essential 
insights in the value chain. Hence strengthening the overall approach to performing operational due diligence.  
3.3. Operational Determinants 
Five overall determinant groups, which are further divided into 18 operational determinants, have been 
identified based on an extensive analysis of the extant literature, which is used to understand the fundamental 
operational determinants influencing the acquisition decision for private equity firms in the due diligence 
phase. 
People & Organization 
The first determinant group concerns people and organization, which is considered one of the most 
important aspects of due diligence. In extension, the process of conducting people and organizational due 
diligence is used to ensure that rhetoric equals reality. Furthermore, organizational restructuring and headcount 
rationalization are considered to be one of the main cost reductions potentials, making the people and 
organizational aspects of the operational due diligence essential (Recardo, 2014). Building on the extant 
literature centered around people and organizational assessment, People & Organization element is split into 
four fundamental determinants: Capabilities, Organizational Structure, Personnel Infrastructure, and Corporate 
Culture, which is used to assess the acquisition candidate.  
 
The first operational determinant concerns competencies and is used to assess the employees’ professional 
experiences and educational background, e.g., through examination of bios and CVs (Recardo, 2014). 
According to Alvarez (2007), it is important to have the right management in place with competencies such as 
operational excellence and leadership skills to establish support throughout the organization. Far too often the 
management team is replaced after the acquisition due to unsound executive decisions. Consequently, Recardo 
(2014) suggests to categorize the management team and senior management team in four categories; i) Strong 
performer, must retain; ii) Okay in present job, but needs development; iii) Should be in job with less scope, 
or iv) Release immediately, as this process creates clarity and transparency for the acquirer. Another approach 
to assessing the management capabilities is proposed by Grebey (2012), who suggest that the executive team 
is evaluated individually on their functional expertise and later compiled to provide a collective score of the 
skill level. This methodology yields specific deficits in the collective and individual management skill-base. 
In addition, Ahammad (2013) refers to managerial talent to be one of the most significant drivers of acquisition 
success which should be evaluated thoroughly in the pre-acquisition phase.  
 
The second operational determinants concern target company’s organizational structure. The acquiring firm 
must assess the company’s organizational structure to understand the mechanisms and dynamics to evaluate 
the target company’s organizational strengths and weaknesses (Ahammad, 2013). The underlying variables 
suggested assessing organizational effectiveness comprise headcount, job classification, roles of 
departments/functions, responsibility distribution, reporting lines and decision-making processes. Grebey 
(2012) emphasizes the importance of evaluating the organizational structure carefully in cases where the 
acquisition candidate itself has grown inorganically, or other corporate events have led to imbalances and 
complexity in the structure.  
 
Thirdly, the personnel infrastructure forms the system in which organizations individuals are managed 
through control of payment, benefit models and human resource services (Grebey, 2012). The personnel 
==PAGE:25==
 
C. Porsgaard et al. / Journal of International Management (2018) 
 
 
Master Thesis: Operational Due Diligence Framework 
25 
infrastructure is assessed as it has a direct impact on the expected deal drivers. For instance, the cost of for 
employee benefits poses a critical factor to be comprehensively analyzed, as this component may hide 
unaccounted for costs (Recardo, 2014). Grebey (2012) suggests a thorough assessment of the entire employee 
compensation package, including base salary, bonuses, educational reimbursements, company vehicle, stock 
options, etc. An uneven and inconsistent compensation model will in some cases pose a significant risk as it 
is difficult to redeem compensation components and to avoid surprising cost or legal issues as it may if not 
assessed in the operational due diligence. According to Grebey (2012), employee training programs should be 
assessed as is a key contributor to efficiency and supports compliance operations in regulated industries. In 
line, Recardo (2014) underlines the importance of understanding training and employee competency 
development in the organizational assessment as it is an essential determinant of employee performance. 
Lastly, both Grebey (2012) and Recardo (2014) accentuate the criticality of evaluating the process of hiring 
and firing employees, including the systems and degree of standardized approach to the process.  
 
The fourth operational determinant is affecting the final acquisition decision and is the target company’s 
corporate culture. According to Grebey (2012), the corporate culture is an essential element in the overall 
acquisition success. In addition, multiple transactions fail due to neglection of cultural challenges or 
insufficient due diligence of organizational adaptability (Recardo, 2014). As acquisitions to a relative extent 
represent a cultural change either in the form of cultural changes to support a turnaround or growth scenario 
which might impact the future operational routines, it is crucial to assess and understand the organization’s 
ability to change. Furthermore, both Grebey (2012) and Shaughnessy (2009) point to the significance of 
understanding employee motivation. As motivation is closely linked to the individual’s performance and 
hereby the target company’s performance, it is essential to substantiate whether the employees are motivated 
and to excel and capable of handling potential changes in the post-acquisition phase. 
Improvement Potential 
The second determinant group is focusing on the improvement potentials. Assessing and quantifying the 
potential upsides of a target company is a central decision component in the question of whether or not to 
acquire a company (Shaughnessy, 2009). The due diligence should help assess the target company’s value 
gains potential with an associated identification of value drivers and a sensitivity analysis (The Boston 
Consulting Group, 2015). The determinant group concerning Improvement Potential is divided into two 
underlying determinants, namely Rationalization, Consolidation, and Efficiency.  
 
The first determinant concerns rationalization and restructuring and is considered a significant contributor 
to cost reduction potentials which underlines the importance of its inclusion in the operational due diligence 
(Recardo, 2014). Such potentials should be investigated to identify opportunities to eliminate duplicate 
functions, overlapping roles or redundant personnel - hence reducing the target company’s headcount, 
impacting the cost base. Alvarez (2007) suggests that an opportunity to identify a rationalization potential lies 
within a reduction of obsolete SKUs or by controlling imbalances in inventory levels.  
 
The second contractual determinants address the efficiency potentials in the target company. Opportunities 
for efficiency improvements pose a crucial opportunity to reduce the cost of goods sold, boost overall supply 
chain performance, and present revenue loss from, e.g., unavailable products (Alvarez, 2007). This entail that 
all supply chain components such as planning, procurement, manufacturing, and warehousing are considered 
for potential efficiency gains. General services and administration is an area which poses an opportunity for 
either technology utilization or outsourcing, leading to a more cost-efficient solution, hence an area to be 
assessed (Rothenbücher, 2008). 
Scalability 
The third determinant group, scalability, include three operational determinants. According to Emerald 
Group (2004), thorough due diligence can identify the target company’s competencies, capabilities, and 
technologies which are used to determine the investment requirements defined to pursue a strategic growth 
direction - hence understanding operational limitation for scaling. 
Morrison (2008), highlights two significant issues which should be discovered in operational due diligence 
to avoid scalability problems in the post acquisitions phase. Firstly, an executive decision in the target company 
hindered its ability to support the outlined growth projections. In this case, the management chooses to 
==PAGE:26==
 
C. Porsgaard et al. / Journal of International Management (2018) 
 
Master Thesis: Operational Due Diligence Framework 
26 
 
26 
outsource essential capabilities which post a risk that led the deal to break. The second issue concerns a 
discovery of growth-related investment requirement that can obstruct the target company’s ability to grow. 
Another related perspective of scalability concern is underlined by Shaughnessy (2009). A thorough 
assessment of the current condition of assets such as facilities, machinery, tooling, etc. is crucial to ensure 
adequate capital allocated to either replacement or restoring. Hence ensuring appropriate conditions for future 
scale or turnaround. Herd (2004) supports the importance of understanding the capital requirements in the 
post-acquisition phase. E.g., some acquisition targets underinvest in physical capital and maintenance, 
meaning further investments are required after the acquisition.  
Tompkins (2012) propose a supply chain perspective to growth constraints, defined as limitations or 
interferences within the target company’s supply chain. He presents ten constraints which should be assets 
when conducting the operational due diligence, and these are as follows: 
 
• 
Supplier Constraints 
• 
Market Constraints 
• 
Financial Constraints 
• 
Company Policy and Cultural Constraints 
• 
Facility Constraints 
• 
Technology 
• 
Staff Constraints 
• 
Management Constraints 
• 
Regulatory Constraints 
• 
Time Constraints 
  
Tompkins (2012) thoughts on supplier dependencies and their ability to supply required goods pose a critical 
constraint. This requires that the target company is not limited by supplier capacity. Hence suppliers can scale 
with the target company, or additional suppliers can be added. Besides capacity constraint is the supplier’s 
ability to deliver on time and at a competitive cost price. Furthermore, Tompkins (2012) suggest that the 
potential identified growth constraints are used to question the acquisition viability and/or leverage the 
financial prospect to outbalance the future cost of the supply chain constraint. 
Information Technology & System Support 
The fourth determinant group embraces information technologies and the systems that support these. The 
traditional term of the group who manages the maintenance of the computers, servers, other hardware and 
software applications, and the internal network is the information technology (IT) group. Information 
management infrastructure includes both the traditional IT function, but furthermore; it includes the systems 
that handle all the data, policies, and procedures. Together these electronic tools, data, and processes automate 
and documents the business functions such as HR, security, engineering, accounting, etc. and are crucial for 
the companies to operate (Grebey, 2012).  
According to Grebey (2012), an extensive assessment that investigates the business’s Electronically Stored 
Information is an essential element during operational due diligence. Recardo (2014) underline the importance 
of a robust investigation and states that a minimum of information needs to be secured. For instants, an HR 
diagnosing require following five item to be answered: 
 
• 
List of IT-systems 
• 
Purpose and type of each system 
• 
Purchase data and contract, including all related fees and change orders 
• 
Description of system support services (in-house, vendor) 
• 
Description of the functions of the tool and of the data elements within IT 
 
The proposed list and assessment ensures that potential unforeseen risk or costs are highlighted in the pre-
acquisition phase, hence avoiding later stages issues. 
Due to the complexity of IT organizations, a change generates exceptional long lead-time. Hence IT is one 
of the crucial areas to cover doing operational due diligence (Morrison, 2008). Serial dealmaker companies 
emphasize having a due diligence team with a range of IT experts to ensure the target company’s IT system 
and IT operation models are investigated minutely.  
==PAGE:27==
 
C. Porsgaard et al. / Journal of International Management (2018) 
 
 
Master Thesis: Operational Due Diligence Framework 
27 
Since most companies have supporting tools for manufacturing or software development the information 
management system carries a high-cost risk for investors in extra expenses, for updating software. According 
to Ahammad (2013), an IT compatibility assessment absent can cause high cost of software conversion and 
often result in a misleading evaluation of the target company. This is one of the frequently mentioned obstacles 
for post-acquisition success.  
Grebey (2012) make a point of highlight that security and policy of the target company’s data need to be 
protected and observed. As information is the source of a company’s knowledge and activities, these must be 
managed correctly. Many different operational determinants are discussed in the literature, but three 
operational determinants are widespread; Data, System, and Tools. 
Operating Cost and Assets 
This determinant group encompasses two closely linked financial measures, namely Operating Cost and 
Assets. These two operational determinants include many underlying features that need to be assessed. The 
operating costs are connected to activities and capital is fixed assets, which by release can benefit to the balance 
sheet. The two elements are closely related, as treating one of the factors reflects on the other (Recardo, 2014). 
E.g., selling a manufacturing plant release a considerable portion of capital, but also cut maintenance costs as 
this activity is no longer necessary. 
 
An organization possesses many different activities, common for the activities is that they either add value 
or cost to the product. Value-adding activities are essential for the core product since they are adding value for 
the customer. Even though, the cost-adding activities compose the majority of activities (Recardo, 2014)). 
According to Clayton (2011), cutting costs is part of one of two reasons for acquiring a company, but 
emphasize that in these cases CEOs are often unrealistic of the potential. Even though, research by PWC 
reveals that the most important lever to value creation is operational cost reduction according to the 
respondents (Roberts et al., 2016). 
 
Anklesaria (2007) present different cost activities, which are grouped into a Direct material, Direct labor, 
Machine and process costs, Material handling, Quality costs, Tooling costs, Facility costs, Management costs, 
General and administrative costs, and Selling and distribution costs. By utilizing the structured technique, 
activity-based costing(ABC), the cost drivers can be identified, and root cause can be realized (Recardo, 2014). 
The ABC method analysis the relationship between the performance of an activity and assign the cost of it to 
an associated resource. Based on this the activities are segregated into a structured architecture that helps 
understanding the cost model and thereby the operating value (Cooper & Kaplan, 1991). 
As mentioned, capital efficiency is closely related to cost structure. While the literature has a great focus on 
cost structure, capital efficiency is more or less neglected. According to Alvarez (2007) managing working 
capital efficiently is an underestimated element. Working capital represents the current assets minus the 
liability of a company and is an expression of the company’s liquidity (Preve & Sarria-Allende, 2010). To 
understand how capital is bound to the acquisition candidate, the assessment should include inventory, 
payables, and receivables are managed (Alvarez, 2007). Recardo (2014) states that hard assets, such as 
factories, facilities, warehouses, etc. reveal the most substantial single potential area to cut costs and free up 
capital, hence underlying the importance of understanding capital allocation and the cost base. 
Risk 
This determinant group appears in most of the literature regarding operational due diligence and M&A. 
There is always, to some extent, a risk associated with the decision-making, hence risk associated with 
corporate decision-making. Across functionalities and determinant groups risk occurs. Tompkins (2012) define 
risk as the peril of a loss and address the operational risk in four categories:  
 
• 
Materials, assets or goods 
• 
Capacity  
• 
Customer satisfaction  
• 
Financial performance 
The operational due diligence is the tool for examining both operational and management issues and risks, 
hence giving a clearer picture of the acquisition potential (Herd, 2004). If major risks emerge during the due 
diligence, it has an impact on the deal in one and/or two ways. Firstly, the acquirer evaluates the criticality and 
==PAGE:28==
 
C. Porsgaard et al. / Journal of International Management (2018) 
 
Master Thesis: Operational Due Diligence Framework 
28 
 
28 
the decision whether to continue or exit the deal. Secondly, if it is chosen to continue or the risk is less 
significant, the acquirer leverages the deal through negotiation, hence mitigating potential risk. Due diligence 
success depends on early detection of major risk elements prior to the acquisition. Both Herd (2004) and 
Tompkins (2012) advocate for evaluating the probability and the magnitude of the risk. This helps to create an 
overview of the risk to establish a prioritized mitigation plan. The element of including risks identification in 
the operational due diligence, across all functional areas and process, is shared by Grebey (2012). 
Through the extensive analysis of the extant literature on operational due diligence, it was possible to 
identify six determinants groups with 18 underlying operational determinants. Each of the operational 
determinants is considered to have a significant influence on the acquirer’s acquisition decision. Table 5, 
summarize the resulting operational determinants.

4. Interview Findings 
Through the practitioner interviews, significant findings which influence the acquisition decision were 
identified. The coding analysis of practitioner interviews resulted in the initial identification of 222 operational 
determinants. After consolidation and a duplicate-removal process of the initial determinants, 27 unique 
operational determinants remained. The operational determinants are classified into six groups based on 
characteristics (similarity and context), which clarify the understanding of the determinants. The attendance 
rate for both the individual determinants and the average for each group are represented in Table 6. The table 
lists operational determinants with highest attention rate first.

The coding analysis and associated attention rate from the practitioner interviews point to Cost & Capital 
as being the most important group of operational determinants, with an average attendance rate of 89%. 
Furthermore, both Net Working Capital and Capital Expenses were accentuated by all advisors and acquirers 
as being most important to cover in the operational due diligence process. The practitioner’s immense focus 
on understanding the operational cost breakdown, cash conversion cycle, and opportunities to free up cash is 
twofold. Firstly, an acquisition is fundamentally tied to the specific investment case. This means the cost 
structure and capital deployment must be understood and incorporated in the final investment case, which 
requires sufficient understanding in this group of determinants. Secondly, an initial analysis of the cost 
drivers and capital efficiency helps develop hypotheses and aim investigation efforts to the most important 
areas to comprehend. In addition, from the perspective of an acquirer, this determinant group helps leverage 
the acquisition negotiation and determine the final acquisition decision. 
==PAGE:30==
 
C. Porsgaard et al. / Journal of International Management (2018) 
 
Master Thesis: Operational Due Diligence Framework 
30 
 
30 
 
The determinants of the group Planning, Efficiency & Quality, receive significant attention from both 
advisors and acquirers (76% of practitioners). The group accounts for many of the determinants and includes 
classical operational elements, such as assessing the target company’s Efficiency & Flow, Capacity 
Utilization Degree, Service Levels, etc. (Russell & Taylor, 2016). Several of practitioners attribute these 
operational determinants as having significant potential for performance improvements gains, which 
strengthens the group’s high average attention rate. 
 
The majority of the interviewees (91%) mention Leadership as a crucial aspect to be evaluated during the 
operational due diligence process. This determinant has a profound impact on the target company’s overall 
execution strength and consequently on the realization of future strategy and improvement potentials. While 
all advisors (100%) accentuate Organizational Structure as a determinant that needs to be covered, only 40% 
of acquires mention this determinant. The advisors pointed toward Organizational Structure as an element 
that supported effective and standardized process for division of tasks, roles, and responsibilities. Lowest on 
the list of People & Organization determinants is Corporate Culture, which gets notable low attention 
(27%). One of acquirer mentioned the Corporate Culture determinant being assessed as a separate area of 
investigation, which could explain its low attention. The determinant group, People & Organization, is 
considering as being assigned medium attention. However, this perspective can be misleading due to the 
major dispersal between individual determinants in the group.  
 
The Technology & IT System group have an average attendance rate (48%), which express the possibility of 
performing a separate IT due diligence. More specifically, Technology & IT System is deprioritized by 
acquirers, and the overall attendance rate is only 33%. Some acquirers and advisors consider an IT 
assessment as a separate due diligence process, which pose a risk of neglecting its influence on the target 
company’s operational setup and supply chain. On the contrary, advisors emphasize both Technology 
Utilization and IT Systems Maturity to a high degree, which is associated with the advisor's more operational 
insights. 
 
The advisors put great focus on the group Data & Documentation. Multiple advisors highlight Key 
Performance Indicators as an essential historical indicator for the target company’s performance. 
Furthermore, it is affiliated with Process & Project Documentation, as both determinants help to understand 
the target company’s ability to reach milestones and achieve strategic goals. The acquirers’ little attention to 
the group as a whole, which leaves the group as Data & Documentation with an average attendance rate 
(48%). 
 
The remaining determinant group Politics & Regulations receive the lowest average attention rate (20%). 
Even though it is assigned low attention, some practitioners mention the operational determinant Approvals 
& Certifications as being a vital component of investigation as it comprises a potential deal breaker. 
However, it is referred to as infrequent and highly case-specific. The same applies to the other operational 
determinants, and especially for Governmental Restrictions and Corporate Social Responsibility that both 
only gets low attention from the practitioners. 
 
The practitioners emphasize another set of overall drivers, which should be considered across all the 
determinant and determinant groups. These driver’s influence is considered to have a major impact on the 
operating model, but they are too high level and generic to be considered as individual operational 
determinants. These drivers are scalability, risk, maturity, potentials, and synergies and cannot be 
comprehended by single analysis points but should be analyzed in the entire operating model to achieve the 
best insights.  
 
All practitioners perceive the organizational scope of operational due diligence as the classic definition of 
supply chain components, as defined by Russell & Taylor (2016). It is interpreted that a key difference in the 
attention rate difference between acquirers and advisors is assigned to the formers generalist knowledge 
==PAGE:31==
 
C. Porsgaard et al. / Journal of International Management (2018) 
 
 
Master Thesis: Operational Due Diligence Framework 
31 
profile, whereas the advisors tend to be more specialists on operations and supply chain related topics. This 
might cause the relatively large differences in attendance rates for individual operational determinants. 
5. Comparative Analysis 
This section analyzes the coherence and the disparities between the extant literature and the empirical data 
obtained in the practitioner interviews. By comparing the findings from the extant literature and practitioner 
viewpoints on operational due diligence a more thorough understanding of the subject is established. The 
initial finding relates to the knowledge gap between literature and practitioners. This gap is expressed 
through two distinct overall difference. Firstly, the depth of the knowledge on operational due diligence is 
very scarce and coverage of the subject is highly fragmented. Secondly, the comparative study reveals areas 
such as Quality Management and Health & Safety which seems non-existing in the extant literature, while 
they were emphasized by practitioners. As stated by Morrison (2008), the emphasis has been put on the other 
due diligence tracks such as commercial, financial, and legal, while neglecting the operational dimension. 
However, the comparative analysis establishes a reasonable relation between the extant literature and the 
practitioner interviews. While the empirical data embrace the subject in a high level of detail and employ a 
practically oriented approach to operational due diligence, the nature of the extant literature expresses an 
incomprehensive research quantity that falls short in the level of detail. The comparative analysis is divided 
into two parts, namely a comparison of the organizational scope and the operational determinants. 
5.1. Organizational Scope of the Operational Due Diligence 
The extant literature describing the organizational scope of operational due diligence is cursory and a 
subject which most clearly is expressed by Recardo (2014). He proposes the value chain perspective which is 
directly comparable with the practitioner’s definition of the organizational scope. Furthermore, Grebey 
(2012) accommodate the supply chain-oriented scope of operational due diligence, which accommodates a 
holistic and comprehensive procedure for approaching the due diligence process. This perspective of the 
operational due diligence is closely related to the fundamental understanding of a company’s operational 
setup (Russell & Taylor, 2016). 
5.2. Operational Due Diligence Content 
People & Organization 
The importance of comprehending a target company’s organizational setup and the individuals involved in 
the operations are extensively and comparably covered in the literature and by the practitioners. The people 
competencies determinant is however considered differently. While the literature contemplates the people 
competencies from a unified organizational perspective, the practitioners express a clear distinction between 
executive and board leadership competencies and workforce capabilities. The practitioner’s different views 
are reasoned with the acquirer’s extensive focus on the leadership’s strategic understanding, ability to execute, 
and domain experience.  
Planning, Efficiency & Quality 
When considering the Planning, Efficiency & Quality determinant group, it is evident that the empirical 
data supplements the extant literature with several add-ons within the fundamental operations management 
area (Russell & Taylor, 2016). The most significant gap between the extant literature and the practitioner's 
view is found to be the incorporation of elements such as quality management and operational planning. The 
literature simply does not put fundamental operations management elements in relation to the process of 
performing operational due diligence. This is a consequence of the limited amount of research on the subject, 
hence the purpose of this paper.   
Cost & Capital 
The depth of the literature is simultaneously evident when comparing the determinant group Cost & Capital. 
While the practitioners break down the cost structure, capital efficiency holds the operating model against the 
==PAGE:32==
 
C. Porsgaard et al. / Journal of International Management (2018) 
 
Master Thesis: Operational Due Diligence Framework 
32 
 
32 
profit and loss statement and the balance sheet, the literature remain very generic. Another difference is 
expressed in the practitioner’s strong focus and coupling between operational findings and the financial impact. 
Data & Documentation 
The literature does not highlight this determinant group as an investigation point of the operational due 
diligence process. In contrary, the practitioners emphasize the importance of using data and documentation to 
understand the status quo and progression of the target company.  
Operational Drivers 
The majority of the extant literature presents a narrowed and limited focus on areas such as scalability and 
operational risk, whereas the empirical data showed that these drivers are essential to investigate across the 
operations and supply chain. However, Tompkins (2012) share the holistic approach to understanding potential 
risks, and the target company’s ability to grow. 
The practitioners introduced the measure of the target company’s current state, namely operational maturity. 
This driver helps address the fundamental state of the operating model, which is considered essential 
assessment approach. This driver was however non-existing in the literature. Lastly, both the literature and the 
practitioners both mentioned improvement potentials as an unavoidable way of examining the validity of the 
acquisition decision. 
6. Introducing the Five Operational Drivers 
Based on the findings and learnings from the literature and the interviews in phase one, it is determined to 
introduce a set of overriding operational drivers. These drivers provided a frame of reference for the 
operational determinants and are characterized as holistic and hypothesis-oriented factors which reaching 
across all the determinant groups. Figure 1. illustrate the relationship between drivers, determinants, and 
analyses. Furthermore, the figure indicates how detailed the knowledge level of the target company needs to 
be, and how case specific the factors are.  
 
Figure 1: The relationship between drivers, determinants, and analyses. 
 
The operational drivers represent a set of key guiding hypotheses for the process of conducting operational 
due diligence.  The five operational drivers are listed and explained below: 
 
1. Operational Maturity: A measure of the target company’s current operating state and its level of 
operational resilience relative to its size, industry, operating model, etc. It is typically measured on 
several attributes and focused around core processes. 
2. Improvement Potentials: A measure of the target company’s existing and/or new improvement 
opportunities. An improvement potential is in this relation considered as a direct financial gain or a 
performance increase.  
3. Scalability: A measure of the target company’s capacity levels and ability to support growth projections. 
Furthermore, the scalability element helps identify potential constraints and future initiatives required 
to support the strategic outlook. 
==PAGE:33==
 
C. Porsgaard et al. / Journal of International Management (2018) 
 
 
Master Thesis: Operational Due Diligence Framework 
33 
4. Operational Risks: This driver concerns the identification of current and potential occurrences that 
might hinder the delivery, trigger a financial claim, prompt reputational damages, etc. Operational risks 
are typically characterized by their probability and degree of severity. 
5. Synergies: This driver is considered in merger cases to describe effects, where the merged company 
will be greater than the sum of the separate individual parts. The synergistic effects are typically 
expressed in a financial or a performance benefit. 
 
These operational drivers are prospectively used as the main point of reference when approaching the 
operational due diligence process.  
7. Survey Findings 
This section concludes the findings from the survey questionnaire. The three main objectives of the survey 
are to 1) validate the supply chain approach, 2) verify the operational deal drivers, and lastly 3) identify the 
most relevant analyses to include. Table 8, comprise all the results of the survey. 
7.1. Supply Chain Perspective 
When answering the survey, the respondents were asked to indicate what areas of the supply chain they 
included in their operational due diligence. Table 7, shows the survey results split into acquirers and advisors.  
Table 7: Results on supply chain perspective

When performing an ODD, the respondents incorporate all four aspects of the supply chain. However, the 
most frequently assessed area is Warehousing & Inventory. This finding aligns with interview findings, where 
the Warehousing & Inventory area of the supply chain often pose the single greatest opportunity to free-up 
capital via stock level and assortment rationalization.  
 
Conversely, Transport & Logistics is considered the least of the supply chain components in 
operational due diligence. However, there is a considerable gap between the acquirers and advisors meaning 
of Transport & Logistics inclusion in an ODD. Transport & Logistics could be considered part of the Sourcing 
& Procurement component if the distribution setup is handled by a third-party-logistics (3PL) provider. When 
a 3PL provider handles the distribution, the discussion often becomes a matter of supplier management, the 
target company’s distribution setup as it is outsourced. Secondly, the acquirer perspective is much more 
investment case focused and generalistic, whereas the advisor has an in-depth and operational focus - hence 
there might simply be a different understanding of Transport & Logistics importance. 
 
Both Sourcing & Procurement and Production are assessed by three-quarters of all the respondents. 
This substantiates the interview conclusion understanding to what degree the target company can procure the 
right amount of goods at a competitive price, as well as being able to produce quality products efficiently.  
7.2. Operational Deal Drivers 
The respondents were asked to indicate which of the five operational deal drivers they put most emphasis 
on and whether additional drivers were included.  
To quantify the individual driver’s importance against each other, a relative importance score was 
calculated based on the result of the survey. The total score across the drivers was divided by the total score 
(1),
