(1) 
All respondents except one, consider the operational drivers as relevant to assess in an ODD. However, one 
respondent only pointed to including Operational Risks and Synergies. In relation, insufficient assessment of 
risks and overestimating synergies have been considered the most influential reasons for unsuccessful mergers 
(McLetchie et al., 2010). As one advisor highlights, the assessment of synergies is very case dependent and 
primarily included in merger cases.   
More than half of the respondents (61%) indicate that Improvement Potential is the essential driver to 
include in the assessment of the target company. This result corresponds well with the primary objective in 
most investment rationales and M&A case, namely to increase value (Goedhart et al., 2017). As the 
Improvement Potential deal driver, specifically focus on identifying the target company’s existing and/or new 
improvement opportunities - where improvement opportunities refer to either a direct financial gain or a 
performance increase. 
The second-most emphasized deal driver to assess it Operational Risks and as one respondent express 
“…Basically, it is all about risk mitigations!”. Both the extant literature and the practitioners state that risk is 
crucial to identify in the early stages of the operational due diligence process. Depending on the risk severity 
it is often an indicator whether to proceed or call off the acquisition process. In continuation hereof, the 
identified risks can be utilized as leverage in the deal negotiation. However, this is only relevant, if mitigating 
opportunities exits and the case can carry the potential burden.  
Assessing the target company’s ability to grow the operating model receives third most attention among 
respondents. The majority of acquirers argue that most acquisitions are growth cases, which emphasizes the 
vitality of understanding scalability since a company’s expansion plans can be slowed or completely hindered 
by physical constraints such as facility limitations or other limitations such as governmental restrictions 
Tompkins (2012). 
Little attention is, however, put toward assessing Synergies, which corresponds with the larger focus on 
acquisitions derived from the practitioner interviews.  
Finally, the lowest scored deal driver is Operational Maturity. Respondents argue that understanding both 
the potentials and risks cover the same elements as maturity disclose and might indicate how the operational 
deal drivers imbricate and can be hard to distinguish between.

However, the most frequently occurring analyses are presented in table 8, where more than 50% of the 
surveyed M&A practitioners apply these in their operational due diligence. A further elaboration on the most 
common analysis are found in chapter 8.

8. Normative Framework 
During the process of this research, the need for a structured and guiding framework emerged from the 
interviews. This is reasoned with the limited research on the subject and the aim of creating a more unified 
and supportive approach to encapsulate the operational due diligence processes. The research findings are 
synthesized into a normative framework based on the literature review, interviews, and survey questionnaire. 
The framework is depicted in Figure 2. 
8.1. The Construction of ODD Framework  
The framework helps to answer the essential question in due diligence “Should we acquire the target 
company from an operational perspective?”. To ease the clarity and the usability of the framework, it builds 
on a matrix structure. The horizontal part of the frame use Recardo’s (2014) supply chain perspective, with a 
letter, added to each element (A, B, C, and D). The drivers identified in the literature review and during the 
interviews compose the vertical part of the frame, with a number added to each driver (1, 2, 3, 4, and 5). The 
drivers are arranged based on the practitioners’ best practice of approaching due diligence. Starting with asses 
the maturity of the company, followed by investigating the potentials, and so forth. 
 
Figure 2: The relationship between drivers, determinants, and analyses. 
The five operational drivers have been identified as the main drivers. They help establish an exhaustive 
framework due to the generic and hypothesis-oriented nature of each driver. Specifically, this ensures that the 
framework is applicable across multiple cases, hence providing a normative framework. The framework will 
hereafter be referred to as the ODD Framework. 
9. Framework Analyses 
The core of the ODD framework consists of a series of analyses, which was identified through the expert 
interviews and the survey. Each analysis has a direct relation to the operational drivers and the supply chain 
components.  
==PAGE:37==
 
C. Porsgaard et al. / Journal of International Management (2018) 
 
 
Master Thesis: Operational Due Diligence Framework 
37 
Due to the limitations of this paper, a deliberate decision to only deep dive into five selected framework 
areas which collectively covers the four supply chain components and the operational drivers. In addition, it 
was chosen to elaborate on the specific analyses which have been particularly mentioned throughout the 
research.  
9.1. Maturity – Production & Manufacturing: Performance Measurement 
Performance measurement is the systematic process of collecting, analyzing and reporting information on 
the resourced used, work produced, and the outcome obtained in the production (Upadhaya et al., 2014). 
Measuring the production performance, allows the organization to identify if external- and internal 
expectations are accommodated. Furthermore, it can function as an indicator of bottlenecks and problems in 
the production which needs to be addressed, towards closing the gaps between current and desired performance 
(Diedrick, 1993). 
Performance measurement in the production has different purposes. A two years-study by LNS Research in 
collaboration with Manufacturing Enterprise Solutions Association (MESA) established an overview of the 28 
most applied metrics in manufacturing (Davidson, 2014).  
To evaluate the performance a set of deliberately selected metrics identify exactly where to take action to 
improve performance. These metrics are called key performance indicators (KPIs) (Weber et al., 2005). The 
balanced scorecard introduced by Kaplan, et al. (1993) is an instrument to validate if the KPIs are a coherent 
set of performance measurement reflected in the business strategy. The balanced scorecard builds on four 
critical areas of the firm’s performance elements: Finances, Customers, Processes, and Learning & Growing.  
Organizations ability to use and select the optimal systematic process, with a sufficient metrics to measure 
performance, is more important than ever, due to the complex nature of the ever-changing environment 
(Choong, 2014). In the assessment of a company’s maturity, the performance measurement is a great indicator 
of how mature the company is, as good performance is associated with high maturity (Bitici et al., 2015). As 
enhanced in all interviews with the practitioners, an assessment of the KPIs aligned with the strategy, and the 
historical data on how well the organization has achieved their KPI goals, indicates to a great extent the 
organization’s maturity. 
9.2. Improvement Potential – Inventory & Warehousing: Inventory Turnover Rate 
Inventory turnover rate is an expression of how frequent a company’s inventory is sold and replaced over a 
certain period – typically a year. If a business has a low inventory turnover rate, it indicates that a high amount 
of inventory is placed in supply chain either in the form of raw material, semi-finished goods or finished goods 
(Russell and Taylor, 2011). Furthermore, stocking up on abundant amounts of inventory has a direct 
consequence on the working capital. This means that capital that could have been used to fund new growth 
opportunities or other improvement initiatives are not fully activated. Releasing dispensable capital tied up in 
inventories increases the financial freedom and investment availability used to stimulate further value creation 
in private equity acquisition (Davies, 2014). 
The turnover rate yields a figure where a low turn rate indicates a well-balanced inventory and vice versa 
with a high number, assuming all things being equal. This number is often used to either benchmark internally 
or externally. The internal benchmark can be to other business units or compared to historical performance, 
while external is compared to either peer (competitors) or industry benchmarks. As highlighted by one of the 
interviewed experts, benchmarking can be a powerful assessment in due diligence, however, it should be 
performed with caution as every business is different (Kroes et al., 2014). Davies (2014) emphasizes that there 
are larges variations when comparing a business’s working capital performance across industries, e.g., retail 
versus heavy industries and even within sectors.  
The widespread use of inventory turnover rate when assessing companies in a due diligence process is likely 
associated with the computation simplicity and data accessibility. The metric is computed by dividing the total 
cost of goods sold (COGS), or annual cost of sales with the average aggregated total value of inventory 
throughout the supply chain. Furthermore, the COGS and average total inventory value are found on the 
financial statement and balance sheet. Conclusively, inventory turnover rate is often used as an indicator of 
how effectively a business operates and control their inventory, hence how well they manage cash flow and 
working capital. 
==PAGE:38==
 
C. Porsgaard et al. / Journal of International Management (2018) 
 
Master Thesis: Operational Due Diligence Framework 
38 
 
38 
9.3. Scalability – Production & Manufacturing: Capacity Increase 
Capacity in production is the capability of a worker, machine, work center, plant, or organization to produce 
output for a specific time period. As the definition implies, capacity has many forms (Blackstone, 2013). The 
MBA SKOOL (2017) list four key elements as important when to decide an efficient way to equalize demand: 
a) Raw material, b) Labor, c) Storage, and d) Equipment (3). Bozarth et al. (2013) elaborate on these elements 
by adding three issues that must be considered when making decisions. How capacity is measured, which 
factors effects capacity, and the impact of the supply chain on the organization. Measurements of capacity are 
generally in terms of inputs, outputs, or a combination of both. E.g., ball pen plant that makes ball pens from 
plastic granulate could express its capacity (input) in terms of available production hours each month and the 
number of available square meters of warehouse space, or capacity (output) in the amount of finished goods 
they can produce in a month. Factors which effects the capacity is the number of shifts, employees, lines, etc. 
Other types of factors are product complexity/variety, or conformance quality which leads to extra customer 
service, reworks, etc. Finally, capacity is often dependent key on suppliers and distributors (Bozarth et al., 
2013).  
The majority of the interviewees stated that most operational due diligence cases are growth cases and 
therefore, it is interesting to investigate the production’s scalability. Revealed from the interviews, most 
practitioners have a practical approach to investigate if possible to increase capacity. The approached evaluate 
three strategic areas: a) Facility layout, b) Labor, and c) Capacity Utility. To evaluate the facility layout, a site 
visit at the plant is accomplished. The site visit is based on the principles of the Lean phenomena Gemba 
(Rahani, 2012). By visualizing the site, the advisors generate an impression if the facility layout is optimal 
based on classic facility layout literature (Bozarth et al., 2013).  
The retention and turnover rate are often used to evaluate the labor dependency. E.g. varies the labor 
dependency across industries, that the organization operates within. High technological industries often have 
more complex processes, which require the scarcer resources, skilled workers compared to the unskilled 
workers. The retention rate and turnover rate are calculated by the formula (Bolden-Barett, 2017) 
𝑅𝑒𝑛𝑡𝑎𝑡𝑖𝑜𝑛 𝑅𝑎𝑡𝑒=

(3) 
 
The retention rate and the turnover rate also gives an impression of the culture in the organization. 
Finally, the productions capacity utilization is a ratio used to compare the actual output with the maximum 
potential output (Bozarth et al., 2013).  It calculated on the basic formula  
𝐶𝑎𝑝𝑎𝑐𝑖𝑡𝑦 𝑈𝑡𝑖𝑙𝑖𝑧𝑎𝑡𝑖𝑜𝑛=

The capacity utilization rate expresses the productions ease of scale without outsourcing, production 
expansion or further acquisition (Garcia, 2017). 
9.4. Operational Risk – Sourcing & Procurement: Supplier Dependency 
When acquiring a company, it is crucial to understand the potential risks that can be associated with supplier 
dependency. Some, typically more immature, businesses rely on few suppliers who if they went out of business 
could break the business, as they haven’t performed a risk assessment from a sourcing perspective. This 
supplier dependency risk can often have a direct impact on business revenue if not handled with caution. More 
specifically, the choice between having few or multiple suppliers raised both advantages and disadvantages 
concerning essential business elements such as prices (cost), lead time, supply uncertainty, capabilities, 
capacity, etc. One of the acquirers articulate how important this as is to understand “The worst possible 
scenario is not being able to supply goods to customers…” and he elaborates “...shortage of products have a 
direct influence on the top line, customer satisfaction, and hereby the overall growth”. 
Suppliers are consequently left with the decision to either stick with single supplier sourcing and retain the 
economics of scale cost advantage or they can shift to dual supplier sourcing and mitigate risk (Thornton, 
==PAGE:39==
 
C. Porsgaard et al. / Journal of International Management (2018) 
 
 
Master Thesis: Operational Due Diligence Framework 
39 
2013). However, when examining the supplier base more in-depth, it might be advantageous to segment 
suppliers after the supply type e.g. directs or indirect and further into commodity versus specified (critical) 
components. Hereby it is possible to differentiate and chose single supplier sourcing on commodity type goods 
and attain dual sourcing on more critical components. Furthermore, the critical suppliers can be analyzed more 
in-depth to evaluate whether the evaluation can include a financial “health” test where the liquidity operations 
are examined to understand potential risks. Secondly, a more fundamental approach can be applied by 
understanding the supplier through interviews during the ODD process. Thirdly, it might be beneficial to 
consider the risk of external and typically incontrollable supply chain disruptions such as labor strikes, natural 
disasters, supplier bankruptcy, and political instability. Evaluating whether a company has performed a 
strategic decision of how to mitigate supplier dependency through e.g. dual sourcing is also a way of assessing 
the target company’s maturity in the sourcing and procurement function. 
9.5. Synergies – Transport & Logistics: Vehicle Utilization   
Synergies or consolidation effects are primarily assessed in merger cases where two companies have 
overlapping entities such as factories, organizational functions, assets or distribution networks. A merger case 
often represents a situation where the customer and supplier base change significant, and depending on the 
overlaps this means that the product flows should be aligned. Furthermore, in merger cases, the end-to-end 
supply chain composes a significant potential to increase profit margins, optimize capital deployment and 
increase service levels (Santich et al., 2016) (Wulfratt, 2011). Specifically, these opportunities represent, 
 
• 
Operating costs: In and outbound transportation costs and warehousing costs 
• 
Working and investment capital: inventory assets and capital investments 
• 
Service levels: Delivery quality, inventory availability, and lead times 
 
Consequently, consolidating the distribution network, often provides an opportunity to realize synergy 
effects with limited risks, however, it should be performed with care to avoid supply shortages or service loss.  
When assessing distribution consolidations in merger cases, there four overall data points that help 
understand and quantify the opportunities; 1) Transportation costs, 2) Product demand by geography, 3) 
Warehouse fixed and variable costs and 4) Warehouse capacity levels, according to Sridurongkatum (2010). 
This is supplemented by Wulfraat (2011), who propose that for both customers and suppliers should be equally 
incorporated in the assessment to understand the dynamics of the market, e.g., historical and future growth. 
However, as propounded by M&A advisors, the data limitation in the ODD process, should be accounted for 
as it is often quite limited. Conclusively, a high-level assessment can be leveraged to evaluate potential cost, 
capital and service synergies. 
10. Theoretical and Practical Implications 
This section is divided into two subsections. The first covers the theoretical contribution both addressing 
shortcomings in extant literature and the theoretical novelty. The second section outlines the practical 
contribution and suggests a practical application of the ODD framework.  
10.1. Theoretical Contribution 
This paper contributes to the limited extant research about operational due diligence in M&As. This is 
achieved by accumulating academic and non-academic knowledge on what operational determinants that 
influence an acquisition decision and the associated analyses. The main theoretical contributions are 
summarized as follows: 
• 
Identification of key operational determinants and their influence on the acquisition decision on M&A. 
• 
Structured analysis and comparison of theoretical and practical knowledge on operational due 
diligences. 
• 
Development of a normative framework for the execution of an operational due diligence, based on a 
supply chain perspective, operational drivers and associated analyses.  
• 
Preliminary validation of the ODD framework for M&A. 
==PAGE:40==
 
C. Porsgaard et al. / Journal of International Management (2018) 
 
Master Thesis: Operational Due Diligence Framework 
40 
 
40 
10.2. Practical Contribution 
The paper provides practitioners, including acquirers and M&A advisors, with a framework to assess an 
acquisition target company’s operational setup and supply chain. The framework presents an exhaustive 
approach with five central operational drivers, which all help ensure a holistic procedure. However, the case-
specific nature of operational due diligence entails that the usages should be supplemented with expertise 
within the process of performing operational due diligence comprising deep operational and financial 
understanding. 
ODD Framework Application Procedure 
This study suggests an iterative process where knowledge about the target company is increased in each 
step and finally leading to key insights and the recommendation. The process is divided into 4 steps as 
illustrated in figure 3 and supplemented with a description of each step. 
 
Figure 3. Framework application procedure 
Step 1 – Initialization: The framework approach is initiated with a process of either a) gathering and 
reviewing all provided information and data about the target company or b) compiling learnings from a 
previous iteration. In case of sub-step a), information and data typically consist of annual reports, an investor 
memorandum, and in some circumstances a quick review of the data room. In the case of sub-step b), the 
learnings from the previous process is synthesized. 
 
Step 2 – Hypothesize: Based on the knowledge from Step 1, a set of hypotheses are developed. This is 
where the ODD framework poses a supportive resource. The operational drivers, Maturity, Improvement 
Potentials, Scalability, Operational Risks, and Synergies, provide a directional setting for the hypothesis 
development.   
 
Step 3 – Analyze: The individual hypotheses are subsequently tested by specific analyses. This is where 
the ODD framework supply the practitioner with multiple analyses aimed the most common hypotheses. An 
example of a common hypothesis is; The target company’s key suppliers are not able to support growth 
projections. This hypothesis suggests the usage of an analysis in the supply chain component of Sourcing & 
Procurement and the operational driver Scalability. This indicates that an assessment of the most critical 
supplier’s capacity levels, financial stability and liquidity, historical delivery performance and capabilities 
should be performed. Such an assessment should lead to either a rejection or a confirmation of the 
hypotheses. 
Furthermore, the analyses should preferably be performed with minimum resource efforts and while 
sustaining high momentum, as the due diligence process is immense time pressured. This often requires that 
the practitioners settle with high-level estimations and qualitative data. 
 
Step 4 – Evaluate: After having performed an iteration of analyses, the practitioners should evaluate 
whether sufficient knowledge has been obtained to either draw a conclusion or whether another iteration 
==PAGE:41==
 
C. Porsgaard et al. / Journal of International Management (2018) 
 
 
Master Thesis: Operational Due Diligence Framework 
41 
should be initiated. If a specific hypothesis requires more in-depth knowledge or new hypothesis have 
emerged, the process starts over.  
The need for more iterations should be completely independent of whether the conclusion has a positive, 
negative or a neutral impact on the investment case. This means that the iterations only are a process of 
obtaining more details about the target company and a stronger acquisition decision foundation. Lastly, the 
framework application procedure should be distinguished from the actual process of a due diligence, as 
several iterations will occur within the due diligence process in full. 
11. Discussion 
The case specificity in M&A expressed by practitioners might challenge the usability and purpose of the 
ODD framework. However, both the literature and the practitioners highlight the importance of having a 
structured, hypothesis-driven and tested approach. This is reasoned by the time-pressured and resource 
demanding nature of understanding an entire company’s operating model within a short period. Consequently, 
the operational due diligence process requires fast conclusions the highest possible likelihood and accuracy – 
preferably based on facts and data. Essentially, this paper proposes the ODD framework which starts from a 
generic perspective in terms of the operational drivers and ending with a set of relatively specific analyses, 
hence trying to achieve the best practical applicability. 
Another important aspect learned through the research process, it the need for expertise both with 1) supply 
chain and 2) financials. Firstly, the time and information limiting aspect of an ODD require quick reasoning 
and understanding of the operating model of the target company. Getting that understanding calls for extensive 
experience within supply chain and operations to make fast and adequate conclusions.  
Secondarily, the inclusion of the investor’s (new owner) perspective: What do the conclusions mean for the 
new owner e.g. In there is a production bottleneck, the investor’s perspective should transfer that conclusion 
to a specific quantification of the CAPEX requirement to resolve that challenge. When conducting business 
oriented analyses in general, it will always be difficult to know “what goods look like” or how to evaluate 
whether the individual target is performing good or bad whenever there is a degree of qualitative analysis. As 
mentioned, here it is important to have deep functional understanding and industry expertise to do a qualified 
evaluation.  
 
The extant literature on ODD signifies a large research gap. While the literature recommends a structured 
approach, no theoretical framework exists. The research process in this project has accordingly been 
explorative through literature, multiple interviews, and a survey. However, to test the proposed ODD 
framework, it would be appropriate to apply it to a case, which is considered the next step in the research.  
12. Conclusion 
Over the last decades, the number of M&A have increased, contemporary with rising complexity and 
uncertainty in operation have caused acquirers to put more emphasis on the operational due diligence to help 
strengthen the prospect of success. These circumstances are making a structured approach more relevant than 
ever.  
 
This research focused on the operational determinants influencing the due diligence and the final acquisition 
decision. Through an explorative literature study, interviews, and survey questionnaire the operational 
determinants are investigated to amplify present comprehension of the operational determinants impact. The 
extensive literature review, revealed an inadequate definition of the operational determinants, while the 
approach to assessing the operational due diligence is non-existing. However, from the interviews and the 
survey questionnaire emerged a sporadic interpretation of the operational determinants and no common 
approach among practitioners. Hereby, it was apparent a normative framework with the identified operational 
determinants for the assessment is a necessity, to cope with the complexity and uncertainty of operations. 
 
The findings on operations determinants are subsequently organized and developed into a normative and 
hypothesis-driven framework for the operational due diligence. The ODD framework consists of five 
operational drivers and four central supply chain components. This approach helps establish an exhaustive and 
==PAGE:42==
 
C. Porsgaard et al. / Journal of International Management (2018) 
 
Master Thesis: Operational Due Diligence Framework 
42 
 
42 
structured process where each driver and supply chain components is associated with a set of analyses and 
assessments to address the respective hypothesis.  
 
Additionally, two noticeable results from the study are worth mentioning. Firstly, even though operational 
due diligence is highly case-dependent, the practitioners agree that a supporting framework to enhance the 
comprehension of the case is necessary, whilst the majority only assess the target companies based on previous 
case experience. Secondly, the practitioners express that risk is investigated as core and underlying part of the 
assessment, while the literature present risk mitigation and risk management as a stand-alone assessment. 
 
Finally, the results were related to the practitioners, and an application suggestion was proposed based on 
an iterative sequence for the ODD framework; (i) Initiate DD process, (ii) Develop hypotheses, (iii) Perform 
analyses, (iv), Evaluate outcome, where the fourth step determines the need for more detailed knowledge, 
hence an additional iteration. In conclusion, this paper contributes with novel insights about operational due 
diligence to the research community, M&A advisors, and acquirers, with the aim of improving the M&A 
success rate.  
12.1. Limitation and Future Research 
Even though the research methodologies applied in this paper followed acknowledge procedures for the 
literature review, interviews, and the survey questionnaire, it is important to recognize the limitations of the 
overall study. Literature focused on operational and supply chain analysis with specific relation to operational 
due diligence, M&A and post-acquisition assessments. This procedure could potentially be extended to a more 
general analysis of operations to accommodate the research gap in the subject of operational due diligence.  
Another limitation is associated with the generalization, in both the interviews and the survey, about the 
most important operational determinants and analyses. This research element is proven statistically significant, 
due to the limited amount of empirical data. Additionally, the proposed ODD framework has only been 
validated through three brief workshop sessions. This process cannot substitute an actual validation and test of 
the framework in a real-life M&A process. Consequently, the findings in this paper might not be applicable in 
all M&A operational due diligence processes.  
 
Due to the limited research on operational due diligence, we have found several areas for future research. 
Firstly, it is suggested presented ODD framework is validated through one or more cases combined with an 
iterative framework adjustment process. This would help refine the framework while building a stronger 
empirical foundation. In addition, future research could include enhancing the depth of the ODD framework. 
More depth and detail will help fortify the applicability while establishing more knowledge on how the 
individual key analyses should be performed, including data requests, the mathematical mechanics, site visit 
checklists, interview guides, etc. In continuation, it is suggested to explorer most suitable process and team 
methodology as these two factors have a major influence on the outcome as expressed in the practitioner 
interviews. Lastly, an exploration of industry-specific tendencies could lead to a more industry-specific 
framework and hypotheses e.g. Too high net working capital within consumer goods or too much rework 
within the heavy machinery industry.  
13. Acknowledgement 
The research team would like to express special thanks to the interviewed acquirers and operational M&A 
advisors for taking the time to contribute to the project. These practitioners have provided tremendous insights 
that could not have been achieved only by the means of literature research. Furthermore, the team would like 
to thank the surveyed for their valuable contribution.  
Lastly, special thanks to assistant professor and co-author of the paper Zaza Nadja Lee Herbert Hansen, for 
her constructive feedback and knowledge sparring which have elevated the outcome tremendously.  
 
Without these people, the resulting research could not have been realized – Thanks.

HARVARD 
BUSINESS 
REVIEW 
Master Thesis: Operational Due Diligence Framework 
49 
 
TECHNOLOGY & OPERATIONS 
SECRETS TO A SUCCESSFUL  
OPERATIONAL DUE DILIGENCE IN M&A 
by Mathias Haubjerg, Chris Berg Porsgaard and Nadja Zaza Lee Herbert-Hansen 
[Not published yet] 
 
ver  the last five years, the number of mergers and acquisitions (M&As) have grown by 4% a 
year. Furthermore, in 2017, one of the largest single and most publicly mentioned acquisition 
to date took place, reaching a price point of +50 billion dollars when The Walt Disney 
Company acquired 21st Century Fox. The combination of increasing M&A activities and increasing value 
of single acquisition emphasizes how the M&A industry continues to strive. In addition, this combination 
substantiates how competition among M&A practitioners increase and how investors put more pressure 
on acquirers to deliver a high return on investment. This situation in the M&A industry stresses the 
importance to identify the secrets to success in M&A.  
Taking the increasing competition into account, nine out ten M&A professionals’ point toward 
operational improvements as being the main driver for value creation and contributor to delivering high 
returns. Likewise, operational cost reductions have the highest priority when seeking to expand profit 
margins in M&As. Looking ahead, 91% of M&A professionals expect that operational improvements and 
cost reductions will be the main drivers for value creation in M&A’s in the coming years. 
This dependency on company value creation generated by operational improvements and cost 
reductions, in the post-acquisition phase, signifies the importance of understanding the opportunities to 
drive operational improvements in the pre-acquisition phase. Hence, a thorough assessment of the 
target company’s operating model through the due diligence process in a necessity to compete among 
other M&A actors. However, the complexity of assessing an acquisition target’s operating model with 
multiple manufacturing and distribution sites across the globe, requires a structured and profound 
operational due diligence approach.  
DEFINING AN OPERATIONAL DUE DILIGENCE 
Historically, most due diligence efforts have been used on assessing the financial, commercial and legal 
aspects of the target company. Understanding the target company’s operating model has been 
O 
==PAGE:50==
 
Master Thesis: Operational Due Diligence Framework 
50 
 
50 
secondary and often completely neglected. Fundamentally, the central purpose of the due diligence is 
to help determine whether the acquirer should proceed with the merger or acquisition. Typically, an 
external advisor performs the due diligence and they are responsible for identifying potential risks and 
opportunities for improvement. The due diligence outcome helps the acquirer in the negotiation phase 
as they utilize the findings as leverage to determine an acceptable price point for the target company. 
Practically, the operational due diligence is part of a due diligence broader assessment which 
furthermore belongs in the pre-acquisition phase as illustrated in figure 1. 
 
 
Figure 1: Operational Due Diligence in M&A 
The operational due diligence is typically conducted in parallel with the legal, financial, and commercial 
due diligence as information and insights are shared across the due diligence tracks. The scope of the 
operational due diligence is focused on the target company’s operating model and it is based on the 
classic supply chain and operations management disciplines. 
UNDERSTANDING THE INVESTMENT RATIONALE  
The first thing to understand before engaging in the operational due diligence is the investment 
rationale. Before the due diligence process, the acquirer performs a pre-assessment of the target 
company where the investment case is evaluated, often only based on investment memorandum and 
publically available data and information. From this, the potential acquirer develops an investment 
rationale explaining the logic and justifying the investment case. The investment rationale depends on 
the type of acquirer and the majority fall within one of three categories: 
 
• 
Institutional investor: Pension funds, insurance companies, mutual funds, banks, etc. who’s 
main objective is to manage its customers and/or investors funds. (They often invest indirectly 
in M&A through private equity funds) 
==PAGE:51==
 
 
 
Master Thesis: Operational Due Diligence Framework 
51 
• 
Industrial investor: Operating company either publicly listed or privately held whose primary 
objective is to increase shareholder value. 
• 
Private Equity firm: Investment management firm which primary focus is to maximize returns for 
its investors through acquisitions, e.g., through venture capital, growth capital, leveraged 
buyout, etc. 
 
In addition to the acquirer type, the investment rationale is often based on an overall investment 
reasoning, which are categories into six reasons to pursue M&A. 
 
1. Active investing: Private Equity firms (PE) or buyout funds who acquire a company with the intention 
to introduce strategic initiatives to make the company run more effectively and profitably than 
before the acquisition. When this objective is achieved, they sell off the company to generate a 
return on investment. These investment targets are often acquired either as a stand-alone company 
focusing on organic growth or via a buy-and-build strategy (platform) where in-organic growth is core 
part of the investment rationale.  
 
2. Scaling the business: Establishing scaling opportunities or elevating top-line results through 
inorganic growth. In these acquisitions, synergy effects are a core part of the investment case as 
these leverage the scaling outcome, e.g., merging the factory footprint to decrease operating costs 
or consolidating purchasing efforts to drive a better bargaining power. 
 
3. Building adjacencies: Expansion into highly related or adjacent business area. Acquiring or merging 
with a direct or indirect competitor, or a business which operates within an adjoining space. The 
acquisition would typically result in expansion into new geographical locations, new product 
categories, or new customer segments. 
 
4. Broadening scope: Acquiring a company to obtain a physical product, technology, software or 
expertise such as R&D capabilities. The rationale is that the process of developing such a product, 
technology or departmental expertise is too complicated, time demanding and/or expensive. 
Consequently, the desired asset is easier obtained through a merger and acquisition. 
  
The first four rationales are the most frequently occurring, whereas the last two are bolder and 
infrequent. 
 
==PAGE:52==
 
Master Thesis: Operational Due Diligence Framework 
52 
 
52 
5. Redefining business: Some large organizations can find themselves staling within their business 
area or industry for example due to sudden technology changes. In such cases, an acquisition can 
present an opportunity to make a strategic shift in the business model which can provide 
momentum to a stalling business.  
 
6. Redefining industry: Combining two, not necessarily adjacent business, to achieve previously 
unexplored product or service offerings. These strategic acquisition cases often force the 
competition to re-evaluate their scope of business and bring a first mover advantage to the 
acquirer.  
 
Some of these investment rationales does not necessarily entail the acquisition of a full company, and 
it could also be a patented technology or a business unit within a larger corporation where practitioners 
perform a so-called carve-out. There are multiple underlying investment rationales such as the more 
aggressive acquisition strategy where a competitor is eliminated through acquisition. However, the 
majority is covered by the six presented above and common for them all is the objective to deliver more 
value to the acquiring company. Having achieved a thorough understanding of the acquirer's 
investment rationale brings clarity on how to approach and understand the target company.  This 
comprehension is especially crucial when subsequently defining the hypotheses to be explored and 
tested in the due diligence phase.  
HAVING AN END-2-END SUPPLY CHAIN PERSPECTIVE 
To understand a target company’s operating model and decide on whether to invest millions, the M&A 
practitioner must include the entire supply chain perspective when performing the operational due 
diligence. Inclusion of a limited part of the supply chain may result in neglectance of critical issues such 
as capacity limitations, supplier shortage risks, etc. or the opposite neglectance of opportunities such 
as NWC reduction potential in warehouses. Conclusively, the focus when conducting the operational 
assessment of the company is on the four core components of a supply chain; Sourcing & Procurement, 
Production & Manufacturing, Inventory & Warehousing and Transport & Logistics, excluding Research 
and development activities, as shown in Figure 2. 
 
 
Figure 2: Operational Due Diligence in M&A 
==PAGE:53==
 
 
 
Master Thesis: Operational Due Diligence Framework 
53 
Where to place the most assessment efforts in the due diligence is very case dependent. For example, 
if a target company use a third-party provider of logistics (3PL), the effort in this supply chain element 
might be different from another company with an owned and operated logistics setup. 
Likewise, the effort used to evaluate Sourcing & Procurement activities often depends on the amount 
spent on direct goods and secondarily on indirect goods. If the spend composes a significant cost 
driver, e.g., the product has expensive raw materials such as precious metals, or if costly sub-
components are sourcing, an assessment of these activities will be crucial. The Production & 
Manufacturing element is considered the core of the business as this typically composes the most 
significant cost driver and capital-deployment area. More specifically, the production often has either a 
large number of FTEs with substantial value-adding activities associated and/or capital-intensive 
equipment or machinery deployed. This means that the Production & Manufacturing element is of great 
importance from an improvement and risk perspective. 
 
The assessment of inventory and warehousing activities focus on storage and handling of raw materials 
or finished goods depending on the inventory role in the supply chain. In simple terms assessing 
inventory and warehousing activities is centered around one core questions: Are the right materials and 
products stored in the right location in the right amount? Specifically, assessing whether the product 
assortment, location and amount are aligned with the demand and market requirements. 
Consequently, this supply chain element often constitutes a substantial opportunity to free up capital 
tied to stock and/or increase service levels by aligning assortment. 
 
The last supply chain element is transport and logistics, and the assessment hereof is very dependent 
on whether a target company operates its own fleet or uses a 3PL solution. This is mainly because the 
3PL solution narrows the opportunities to improve the transport operating model compared to an 
owned and operated transport setup. Furthermore, the cost of transport and hereby logistics 
percentage is also very dependent on both the industry and product, where material's weight and 
volume apparently has considerable influence. 
 
The supply chain perspective provides a holistic and exhaustive approach to performing an end-to-end 
assessment of an acquisition target’s operating model. Furthermore, it helps minimize the risk of 
neglecting critical areas of investigating which potentially could influence negatively in the post-
acquisition phase or could have supported the valuation and negotiation process. 
 
 
==PAGE:54==
 
Master Thesis: Operational Due Diligence Framework 
54 
 
54 
Operational Deal Drivers 
After having interviewed and surveyed multiple M&A practitioners, it is evident that five distinct deal 
drivers are used to assess a target company’s operating model. Together with the acquirer’s investment 
rationale, these operational drivers help establish the initial hypotheses to test during the operational 
due diligence. Each of the five operational deal drivers have a pivotal effect on the final acquisition 
decision as they influence the target company’s operating model positively or negatively. They are 
defined as follows: 
 
1. Operational Maturity: A measure of the target company’s current operating state and its level of 
operational resilience relative to its size, industry, operating model, etc. It is typically measured 
on several attributes and focused on core processes. 
 
2. Improvement potentials: A measure of the target company’s existing and/or new improvement 
opportunities. An improvement potential is in this relation considered as a direct financial gain 
or a performance increase.  
 
3. Scalability: A measure of the target company’s capacity levels and ability to support growth 
projections. Furthermore, the scalability element helps identify potential constraints and future 
initiatives required to support the strategic outlook. 
 
4. Operational risks: This driver concerns the identification of current and potential occurrences 
that might hinder the delivery, trigger a financial claim, prompt reputational damages, etc. 
Typically, the character of operational risk is their probability and degree of severity. 
 
5. Synergies: This driver is considered in merger cases to describe effects, where the merged 
company will be greater than the sum of the separate individual parts. Typically, the expression 
of the synergistic effects is a financial or performance benefit. 
PUTTING IT ALL TOGETHER:  
A FRAMEWORK FOR OPERATIONAL DUE DILIGENCE 
Synthesizing the findings into an operational due diligence framework helps M&A practitioners answer 
whether acquiring a target company is an operationally sound decision. The framework consists of the 
four supply chain elements representing the horizontal axis and the five operational deal drivers 
representing the vertical axis, which results in a 5x4 matrix structure and shown figure 3. 
==PAGE:55==
 
 
 
Master Thesis: Operational Due Diligence Framework 
55 
 
Figure  3: 5x4 Matrix Structure 
 
 
 
==PAGE:56==
 
Master Thesis: Operational Due Diligence Framework 
56 
 
56 
Each of the 20 matrix elements are then used as a starting point to formulate the hypothesis that 
subsequently will test throughout the operational due diligence process. The framework contains five 
hypothesis examples. Example hypothesis 1.2 refers to the application of lean principles in the 
production environment in the target company. For this hypothesis, the operational due diligence 
practitioner assesses the maturity and implementation of lean throughout the production which could 
include an evaluation of the usage of core lean principles such as 5S, visual management, whiteboard 
meetings, just-in-time, Kanban, etc.  
Practically, the framework has a dual purpose. The first aims at inspiring practitioners to develop 
hypotheses to understand target company’s operating model both before and throughout the 
operational due diligence process. Secondly, it helps establish exhaustiveness and ensures a 
structured process for the overall operational assessment.  
FIVE KEY OPERATIONAL DUE DILIGENCE VIEWPOINTS 
Due to the time-pressured, confidential and decision-critical nature of a due diligence process, it is 
worth considering how to best support a well-founded and accurate go/no-go decision at the end of the 
process. This section summarizes five key viewpoints, based on good and bad experiences from both 
acquirers and advisors. 
NO TWO COMPANIES ARE ALIKE 
Acknowledging that no two companies encompass the same culture, structure, employees or operating 
model is a must when performing operational due diligence. This diversity means that the M&A 
practitioner’s process must modify the procedures and the hypotheses to the individual target 
company. Approaching operational due diligence with an “off-the-shelf” or standard tool pose a 
significant risk of not catching the deal makers and breakers. Consequently, a set of individualizes 
hypotheses formulated in the initial phase of the due diligence should guide the acquisition process. 
Furthermore, the hypotheses should be updated throughout the entire process to utilize new insights. 
FRONT-LOAD AND PROGRESS ITERATIVELY 
The first 1-2 days in a due diligence process, is often used to set the direction, plan and establish the 
fundamental hypotheses. These hypotheses function as a guiding star throughout the project’s usually 
short time horizon of 3-6 weeks. However, knowledge and information about the target company are 
often insufficient in the beginning and improve toward the end. Consequently, allocating a lot of 
resources in the beginning of the project helps strengthen the initial understanding of the target 
==PAGE:57==
 
 
 
Master Thesis: Operational Due Diligence Framework 
57 
company and with the direction setting. Also, it is crucial to frequently revisit the hypotheses to evaluate 
and continuously steer efforts toward the most vital areas assess. 
SEEK DATA-DRIVEN CONCLUSIONS 
The substantial amount acquirers invest in a single company, often reach multi-million dollar figures. 
Considering these massive investments and the relatively short time horizon to make the acquisition 
decision, stress the importance of reaching an accurate and valid conclusion. Consequently, 
quantitative analyses are often preferred over qualitative analyses as data-driven conclusions are less 
subject to interpretation and bias. Data sources from quantitative assessments are amongst other the 
investment memorandum, the data room, benchmarks, industry reports, and publicly available 
information such as financial statements. However, the qualitative input is a core part due to a limited 
amount of data available in the auction-oriented M&A processes opposite to the acquisition exclusivity 
processes, where more data is available. Effective qualitative sources are 1:1 interviews and 
observational studies.  
FUNCTIONAL AND INDUSTRY EXPERTISE 
Achieving success with performing operational due diligence is highly dependent on the team 
composition and availability of knowledge resources. The expertise can either be a permanent part of 
the team or accessed “on-demand” through interviews, depending on the case. Firstly, profound 
functional experience within each of the supply chain elements is a must to perform effective and 
adequate conclusions. Secondly, experience within the industry in which the target company operates 
established a knowledge foundation and gives an edge for where to focus due diligence efforts. Simply 
put, functional and industry expertise helps develop a frame of reference for the assessment, explicitly 
knowing what good and bad are looking.  
CONTINUOUS FOCUS ON THE INVESTOR PERSPECTIVE 
As an investor, the M&A, and specifically the due diligence process is a constant balance of risk versus 
reward. Throughout the due diligence, findings emerge which often can be boiled down to either a 
potential benefit or a risk that the investor must evaluate. Consequently, it is crucial always to reflect on 
how the finding influence the investment case. Simply put, the assessor must see the operational 
finding through the eyes of an investor. For example, if an assessment of manufacturing setup reveals 
significant torn on machinery, it is essential to transfer this finding into the CAPEX and financial 
perspective that it represents, and how that influence the investment Lastly, it is vital to keep the 
investment rationale as a guiding star for the due diligence direction and efforts. 
==PAGE:58==
 
Master Thesis: Operational Due Diligence Framework 
58 
 
58 
CASE: SAMSUNG’S ACQUISITION OF HARMAN 
The 11th of March 2017 Samsung Electronics (Samsung) announced their acquisition of Harman 
International Industries (Harman). The acquisition is the largest acquisition ever made by Samsung to 
this date, with a purchase value of approximately $8 billion. From an operational perspective, the 
acquisition has received a lot of attention. Samsung has historically made technological acquisitions 
and subsequently build a supply chain around the technology or products. Harman represents a 
company with an already relatively maturely established supply chain, which differs significantly from 
Samsung's previous acquisitions and their operational buy-and-build strategy. As Samsung inevitably 
has put a substantial effort into assessing Harman’s operational setup and supply chain in the pre-
acquisition phase, the operational due diligence framework is theoretically applied. A high level of 
confidentiality and limited available data characterize the M&A activities. This means that several 
assumptions are made when applying the operational due diligence framework to the Harman 
acquisition.  
SAMSUNG AND THEIR INVESTMENT RATIONALE 
To comprehend the investment rationale, the first step is to understand Samsung as an investor. 
Samsung is publically listed and the majority shareholder is Samsung Group, which is a South-Korean 
multinational conglomerate. Samsung operates within IT and electronics and is the largest 
manufacturer and provider of televisions, smartphones, and memory chips in the world. Furthermore, 
they have a worldwide manufacturing and sales footprint, more than 308.000 employees, a turnover of 
more than $190 billion, which position them as a market leader. These elements characterize Samsung 
as being an industrial investor with the primary focus on increasing shareholder value. Furthermore, 
Samsung’s stable market-leading position and their vast manufacturing capability and size within 
consumer electronic units, pose an excellent opportunity to expand into highly related or adjacent 
business areas. Especially in business areas where they can leverage their extensive commercial and 
operational experience within consumer electronics.  
 
The automotive industry’s dependency on software and electronics are increasing, and so are the tech 
companies interest in getting part of the market share. Electronics attribute to around 30% of a modern 
vehicle’s cost. Samsung projects that the automotive electronics segment will grow to more than $100 
billion by 2025. While Samsung has the resources and capabilities to enter the industry, an acquisition 
of a company operating in the industry shortcuts the long time-horizon it takes Samsung to gain a well-
established brand and a market share big enough to compete in the industry, both commercially and 
operationally. The four major players delivering software and electronics solution to the automotive 
==PAGE:59==
 
 
 
Master Thesis: Operational Due Diligence Framework 
59 
industry are Boston Acoustics, Inc., Bose Corporation, Pioneer Corporation, and Harman International 
Industries. The below list summarizes Samsung’s underlying investment rationale into three key areas: 
 
1. Positioning: The acquisition of Harman gives Samsung an immediate position as the tier-1 parts 
supplier to General Motors and Tesla, who are among the most prominent car manufacturers in 
the world. 
 
2. Product portfolio: Harman is associated with premium audio equipment, and quality products. 
Harman’s product portfolio consists of more than twenty robust brands, such as B&O 
automotive, AKG Acoustics, JBL, Infinity, etc. Harman has acquired the main part of them over 
the last fifty years. 
 
3. Knowledgebase: Harman’s massive knowledge resource base makes up more than 8.000 
software engineers working with the cloud-based consumer experience and end-to-end services 
for the automotive market. 
 
Furthermore, the high number of successful acquisitions within Harman indicates robust M&A 
capabilities, specifically within the post-acquisition integration phase.  
THE SUPPLY CHAIN 
The fundamental strengths and weaknesses of Samsung’s operating model and supply chain need to 
be well understood to comprehend potential improvement, synergies and the overall match between 
Samsung and Harman from an operational perspective. 
VERTICAL INTEGRATION 
Samsung’s vertical integration strategy starts upstream where they own several businesses who 
extraction and produce raw materials, whereas they also control downstream activities such as retail 
outlets. Compared to Harman, who has a low degree of integration, Samsung owns the entire supply 
chain and is known for their outstanding supply chain management capabilities. Samsung has a 7-step 
supply chain transformation strategy, and they are known for their operational excellence model which 
builds on core Toyota Lean principles. These capabilities have become one of their core competencies. 
Figure 4 depicts Samsung’s 7-step approach. 
 
==PAGE:60==
 
Master Thesis: Operational Due Diligence Framework 
60 
 
60 
 
Figure 4: 7-step supply chain transformation strategy 
Even though Harman’s supply chain is less integrated and relatively less mature, it constitutes a core 
part of Harman’s operating model as stated by Lalit Panda, SVP of Supply Chain for Harman. "Our 
supply chain is a critical part of our commitment to bringing innovative products to market faster and 
further increasing customer service." 
SUPPLY CHAIN FOOTPRINT 
While Samsung’s headquarter is located in South-Korea and Harman’s in the USA, both companies 
have a global supply chain footprint and customer base. Samsung has 38 production sites located in 
17 countries around the world. Harman operates in 33 countries, and have established manufacturing 
facilities in 11 countries. Harman and Samsung have an overlapping manufacturing and distribution 
footprint in several countries such as the United States, Germany, Brazil, Hungary, China, and India, 
while they complement each other in other regions. Samsung’s manufacturing network, before the 
acquisition of Harman, is shown in figure 5. 
 
 
Figure 5: Samsung’s Manufacturing Network 
HYPOTHESES GENERATION AND ANALYSES 
Thorough understanding of the investment rationale, the preliminary comprehension of Samsung’s and 
Harman’s business model and their supply chain, it is possible to establish a set of initial hypotheses.

in the assessment. For the purpose of this case, there are outlined four key hypotheses based on the 
case information and general supply chain knowledge.

==PAGE:62==
 
Master Thesis: Operational Due Diligence Framework

62 
These four hypotheses in table 1 are high level and would practically have multiple underlying 
hypotheses easing the analysis burden. Furthermore, there would also be several other hypotheses 
ensuring the assessment of all relevant areas of the target company (Harman) are sufficient.  While the 
preliminary hypotheses are based on the investment memorandum and publicly available data, the 
analysis testing the hypotheses are conducted with information from interviews, observation from site 
visits, and data from the data room. Some hypotheses may only require one analysis with limited 
information needed, while others can run the entire due diligence period, with multiple sub-analysis and 
data points. Toward the end of the operational due diligence phase, the outcome of all hypotheses and 
associated analyses are synthesized and structured into a final recommendation.  
 
Conclusively, the importance of the operational due diligence as a core part of M&A processes are 
unneglectable. Hence, this paper proposes the Operational Due Diligence Framework, which is focused 
around a set of influential deal drivers and the end-to-end supply chain perspective. The framework 
forces M&A practitioners to work hypothesis-driven and consider all aspects of the target company’s 
operational setup. The result is a structured and exhaustive assessment of the acquisition targets 
operating model, leading up to a final go/no-go decision.  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
==PAGE:63==
 
 
 
Master Thesis: Operational Due Diligence Framework 
63 
5. Conclusion 
The continuous growth in M&A activities, both in value and numbers, combined with a prolonged 
outlook for operational improvements as the primary value creator, demand a structured and precise 
operational assessment of a target company. However, a framework for what and how to conduct 
operational due diligence remain non-existent, even though M&A has been researched extensively. 
Consequently, the purpose of this thesis was first to identify the operational drivers which influence 
the acquisition decision. Secondly, to develop a structured and normative framework to assist the 
M&A practitioners when performing an operational due diligence.  
 
The results of the research concluded that a holistic and exhaustive approach is a necessity to 
comprehend a target company’s operating model. To meet these criteria, an end-2-end supply chain 
perspective consisting of four elements; Sourcing & Procurement, Production & Manufacturing, 
Inventory & Warehousing, and Transport & Logistics was chosen. Furthermore, 18 determinants 
were identified, and these were synthesized into five overall operational deal drivers. These drivers 
are fundamental for the hypothesis-driven approach and vital to capture the target company’s 
operating levers while evaluating them against the acquirer’s predefined investment rationale.  
 
In parallel, three main findings, related to the due diligence process, were identified as being pivotal 
for an accurate assessment result. Firstly, there is a high degree of case dependency, meaning that 
no target companies are identical. Thus, it is not possible to develop a prescriptive framework for 
how to conduct operational due diligence. Secondly, the time-pressured nature of an M&A process 
requires a hypothesis-driven approach. This approach helps continuous progression, and alignment 
between assessment efforts and the investment rationale. Lastly, it is a requirement to have access 
to in-depth functional expertise within the four supply chain elements, while conducting the 
operational due diligence. Furthermore, it is decisive to have financial knowledge-resources to 
guide the process and ensure accordance between the functional analyses and the overall 
business/investment case. 
 
Conclusively, this paper contributes to the research on M&A and specifically operational due 
diligence by examining the operational key drivers influencing the acquisition decision. The study 
proposes a novel and normative, operational due diligence framework, which helps M&A 
practitioners focus efforts and achieve more accurate acquisition decisions.

Professor and our Research Supervisor, for her valuable and constructive guidance through the

Appendix A – Conference Papers 
Appendix A.1 - EurOMA Conference Paper 
 
A framework for operational due diligence 
 
 
Chris Berg Porsgaard (chpo@implement.dk) 
The Technical University of Denmark 
 
Mathias Haubjerg 
The Technical University of Denmark 
 
Zaza Nadja Lee Herbert-Hansen 
The Technical University of Denmark 
 
 
 
 
 
Abstract 
Operational due diligence is an essential step in the purchase decision process when mergers and 
acquisition (M&A) practitioners assess whether a target company can realize future performance 
expectations. To accommodate the high activity level in M&As and the increasing complexity in 
supply chains, the aims of this paper is to strengthen the current research on operational due diligence 
and provide practitioners with a conceptual approach to perform successful operational due diligence. 
Through a structured literature review and several expert interviews, this paper identifies and 
develops a novel framework for the operational determinants influencing the final acquisition 
decision in M&As. 
 
Keywords: Operational Due Diligence, Due diligence framework, Pre-Acquisition Assessment 
Introduction 
In spite of low success rates in mergers and acquisitions (M&A), the amount of M&A activity has 
steadily increased over the last five years (Institute of Mergers, Acquisitions and Alliances, 2016). 
One of the largest challenges can be traced back to the acquirer's ability to understand operating 
model of the company that is considered for acquisition (target company). Furthermore, early 
identification of the target company’s operational capabilities to contribute to the strategic outlook is 
often insufficient (Christensen, 2011). 
 
The significance of understanding the key drivers in a target company remain a major contributor 
to M&A success (Herd, 2004) as transactions continues to become more wide ranging and 
comprehensive (Picker, 2016). At the same time factors such as complex distribution networks, 
supplier relations and IT systems contributes to increasing supply chain dependencies, which signifies 
==PAGE:70==
 
Master Thesis: Operational Due Diligence Framework 
70 
 
70 
the criticality of understanding operational drivers in an industrial target company (Yogesh Malik, 
2011). The combination of the increasing complexity in industrial companies, and the large M&A 
activity within the industrial sector, indicate the importance of understanding operational risks and 
potentials (Roberts et al., 2016). 
However, the process of planning and efficiently executing an operational due diligence presents 
itself as a challenge even for the most seasoned firms (Recardo, 2014). Consequently, the operational 
due diligence process should yield pivotal insights which help the acquirer validate the target 
company’s operational performance and improvement potentials.   
 
The traditional focus areas in the M&A due diligence process have been on understanding the 
financial and legal aspects of the target company (Grebey, 2012). This leads to sufficient insights in 
the financial statements, assets, cash flows, contractual obligations, etc., but neglected to comprehend 
to operations aspects (Shaughnessy, 2009). The result of neglecting operational insights can 
potentially affect the final acquisition decision. In addition, such knowledge functions as a lever for 
valuation of the target company, price negotiations, and the possible subsequent integration and 
realization of improvement potentials (Herd, 2004). Consequently, it is proposed to allocate 
equivalent resources to obtaining insights in the target company’s operating model, as with the 
financial and legal due diligence (Morrison, 2008). When that is said, the nature of assessing an 
acquisition targets operating model is considered a complex affair. Unlike the financial and legal due 
diligence, the operational due diligence does not rely on accounting principles or the law, which 
emphasizes the importance of having a structured and refined approach (Grebey, 2012). In addition, 
Morrison (2008) suggests that a standardized and tested approach increases the likelihood of 
performing a successful due diligence in terms of comprehension and precision.  
To accommodate this need, the purpose of this paper is to identify the operational drivers 
influencing an acquisition, and subsequently propose a structured approach to performing an 
operational due diligence. The research aims at providing a framework to assist both acquires and 
operational M&A advisors in performing more effective and precise operational due diligences.  
 
For the purpose of this paper, the term operational determinants are defined as factors having either 
a positive or a negative influence on the target company’s operational ability to drive competitiveness. 
The organizational scope includes the Procurement & Sourcing, Manufacturing, Warehousing & 
Inventory and Transport & Logistics, meaning that the functional areas Sales, Marketing and R&D 
is excluded in contrast to Grebrey (2012)’s definition of an operational due diligence. 
 
Methodology 
This research focuses on identifying the key operational drivers used to evaluate whether an 
acquisition should complete. The best approach to perform an operational due diligence is 
simultaneously explored. Due to the complex nature of the research objective, a qualitative approach 
is used to provide rich and in-depth data. The explorative nature of the study allows for thorough 
understanding of the research area. Therefore, the case-study approach is the most appropriate 
research methodology (Yin, 2013).  
 
In total five private equity firms/professionals (acquirers) and six advisors have been selected to 
obtain in-depth data from the perspectives of both parts of the ODD. To gather the data, semi-
structured interviews were conducted. The acquirers were selected based on multiple parameters 
including that they have a) experience with acquiring international companies, b) experience with 
operational due diligence, c) acquired companies with a production of physical goods and a supply 
chain. Likewise, the advisors were chosen based on multiple parameters including that they have a) 
==PAGE:71==
 
 
 
Master Thesis: Operational Due Diligence Framework 
71 
performed more than three operational due diligence, b) financial understanding, c) deep operational 
expertise, within sourcing, 
 transport, manufacturing, and distribution. Common for both parts is 
that they express a need for better understand the operational due diligence process. 
 
The research is divided into four key phases (i) an extensive literature review of 131 research 
papers and industry publications, were narrowed down to 42 by screening process with several 
exclusion criteria. These were further decreased to the final 22 articles by a detailed examination of 
the 42 articles.  (ii) Multiple semi-structured interviews analyzed by a coding and frequency analysis, 
(iii) structured comparison of the literature review and the empirical data, and (iv) development of a 
novel framework that assists acquirers and advisors conducting operational due diligence. 
 
Data from the acquirers and advisors were gathered from their experience with prior operational 
due diligence cases, meaning M&As which have been concluded. The research team worked in close 
collaboration with the advisors, since they are the executing part of the operational due diligence. The 
project is concluded in early spring 2017. Data were collected through interviews, white papers and 
company documents. The explorative approach is applied to obtain key insights through the semi-
structured interviews with the private equity professionals and consultancies. In this manner the 
project ensures a high degree of industry relevance by linking theory and industry practice. 
Furthermore, the method enabled triangularity of the findings between the three sources of 
information, namely extant literature, acquirer interviews and advisor interviews, thereby enhance 
validity. 
 
Literature review 
Through a comprehensive analysis of extant literature focused on operational due diligence, six 
operational groups have been identified. The six operational determinant groups have 18 underlying 
operational determinants collectively. Each of the operational determinants is deemed to have a 
significant influence on an acquirer's acquisition decision. Table 1, displays the resulting operational 
determinants of the rigorous literature review.

Throughout the literature review, it became evident that it was possible to identify a comprehensive 
set of determinants concerning the operating model of a target company.  
 
As proposed by Recardo (2014), the perspective of assessing a targets operational setup is through 
a value chain perspective. This approach helps comprehend the individual components in the target 
company and helps limit the risk of disregarding crucial aspects of the target company’s operating 
model. Furthermore, the organizational scope of the operational due diligence follows Grebey (2012), 
with exclusion of the functional areas Sales, Marketing, and R&D. Definitively, the following four 
functional areas are considered part of the operational due diligence: 1) Sourcing & Procurement, 2) 
Manufacturing, 3) Warehousing & Inventory and 4) Transport & Logistics. For the purpose of this 
paper, this definition is applied to focus the organizational scope of the ODD as illustrated in Figure 
1. 
==PAGE:73==
 
 
 
Master Thesis: Operational Due Diligence Framework 
73 
 
 
Figure 1: Operational Due Diligence Scope 
 
Expert interviews 
A series of expert interviews was performed with acquirers and operational M&A advisors. To 
quantify the relative importance of operational determinants and facilitate the later framework 
development, coding and frequency analysis of the expert interviews was conducted. The coding 
analysis focused on identifying operational determinants accentuated during the expert interviews, 
which led to a compiled identification of 222 operational determinants. These individual determinants 
were subsequently consolidated to eliminate duplicates and unite equivalent determinants. These 
were after several iterations narrowed down to 27 operational determinants, which all are associated 
with a frequency score as shown in table 2. The score represents the frequency of which the individual 
operational determinants was mentioned across interviews. The operational determinants are grouped 
based on similarity and context.
